PHILIPPINES
DEVELOPMENT BANK OF THE PHILIPPINES
Snall and Medium Industry Financing
A.  3nall and Medium Industry (SMI) Lending Programs
1.        Background.  Although DBP has been making small loans for industrial
projects since the establishment of its predecessor, the Rehabilitation
Finance Corporation in 1947, it first instituted a special lending program
specifically for small-scale industries in FY71 in conjunction with the
University of the Philippines Institute for Small-Scale Industries (UPISSI).
In September 1971 DBP launched a countryside development lending program,
part of which was aimed at stimulating the growth of small and medium
industry outside the Greater Manila Area. As DBP's lending to SMI began
to expand in FY73 and the beginning of FY74L, a separate industrial projects
department (IPD II) was formed in November 1973 to be responsible handling
small loans (below P1 million). This change reflects ]BP's awareness that
small-scale borrowers require a different approach with a different kind of
lending relationship from large-scale borrowers. It also enables the staff
of IPD II to concentrate more fully in the special problems of small-scale
industry. Loans above R1 million are handled by IPD I, a department of
proven ability (Annex 3, para. 3). DBP's Branches are also handling an
increasing number of SMI loans. However, Branch Managerst commitment
authority is presently limited to P20,000 (increased from F5,000 in FY73),
so all SMI loans have to be reviewed by either IPD I or IPD II before
submission to the Board of Governors for final approval (para. 15).
2.         Definition.  DBP has adopted the Government's definition of a small-
scale industrial enterprise as one with total assets of between P100,000 and
F1,000,000, and a medium-scale industrial enterprise as one with total assets
of between F1,000,000 and P4,000,000.  DBP has also introduced a Home Industries
Financing Program to cater for firms with assets of less than P100,000. In its
lending operations, however, DBP does not classify its borrowers by size of assets.
An analysis of DBP1s industrial loans during FY72-FY74 shows that on the whole
loans of P50,000 or more have been made to borrowers with assets over P100,000.
Only a very few loans of P50,000 or more have been made to borrowers with assets
under F100,000. Similarly, hardly any loans of under P50,000 have been made to
borrowers with assets over P100,000. At the upp.er level, the analysis showed
that borrowers with assets of under P4,000,000 never availed of loans over
P2,500,000 due to debt/equity constraints. There are, however, a few cases
where firms with assets of over Pl4,000,000 have borrowed amounts of less than
P2,500,000. These cases are easily identifiable. The mission has therefore
decided to use loan sizes of between P50,000 - F1,000,000 and P1,000,001 -
P2,500,000 to classify DBP's small-scale industry and medium-scale industry
lending operations respectively.



ANNEX 4
Page 2
3.        Past SMI Operations.  During the three fiscal years (FY72-74)
since the countryside development program was launched DBP has made 206
loans amounting to P60.4 millioni for SMI as defined above. After a slow
start in FY72, SMI lending increased rapidly in FY73 and FY74 (Appendix 1).
By FY74 the Branches accounted for 45% of the projects and 37% of the amount
lent.  Appendix 7 shows the distribution of SMI approvals by LBP Branches
and Appendix 6 the distribution by industry suo-branches.  In FY74 SMI
lending represenQed about 42% of l BP's total lending to industry compared
with less than 5% in FY72. This rate of increase has been achieved with
virtually no promotional efforts on the part of DBP and despite DBP's
long processing time (para. 14) and stiff collateral requirements (para.ll).
This reflects both DBP's dominant role in financing SNI which has only
recently been challenged by the availability of IGLF financing, and the
large underlying demand for financing by SMI entrepreneurs of which DBP
finances only a small sha-re. Its present collateral and other requirements
discourage all but the fairly affluent and well collateralized enterpreneurs
from approaching it for finance (paras 10 and 11).
4.        Projected Operations.  DBP's SMI operations are expected to
increase very rapidly again in FY75 on the basis of projects already approved,
applications under processing in DBP, and the number of projects being
prepared for DBP financing by the Department of Industry's MASICAP teams
(Volume II, P-rt 2).  DBP'sSMI pipeline is shown in Appendix 5. After FYT75
DBP's SMI operations have been projected to increase only moderately, partly
due to the larger role to be played by IGLF and partly because of capacity
constraints within DBP.  DBP Branches, however, are expected to account for
a greater proportion of DBP's SMI lending as a result of the industrial
dispersal policy and the concentration of DOI/MASICAP efforts in areas
outside the provinces in the vicinity of Manila. Projected SNI lending
operations broken down by size of loan, DBP Branches, and industry sub-
branches are shown in Appendices 1, 6 and 7. In aggregate DBP is projected
to make 531 SMI loans in FY76 and FY77 amounting to P 208.6 million.
Medium-scale industry is expected to account for 6% of the number
of loans and 28% of the loan amount. About half of the SMI lending
is expected to originate in DBP's Branches. Industry distribution is expected
to follow the past pattern of DBP's 31I lending, with food processing,
cement and hollow block manufacturing, apparel and footwear, wood processing,
agricultural machinery, machine shops and foundries accounting for over 60%
of the total.
5.         Industrial Loan Portfolio.  As of June 30, 1974 DBP had a total of
5,277 industrial loans outstanding amounting to F1,382.3 million of which SMI
accounted for P229.4 million (or 16.6%). Principal and interest in arrears
were equivalent to 22% of the total principal outstanding. 74% of the number
industrial loan accounts (accounting for 49% of total principal outstanding)



ANNEX 4
Page 3
have been in arrears for over a year. The table below summarizes the arrears
for industrial accounts since FY72,
Table 1. DBP - Sumary of Industrial Accounts (as of June 30)
FY72        FY73        FM
(percent)  (percent)    percent)
Number of accounts in arrears over
one year/Total number of accounts          86          84           74
Principal and interest in arrears/
Outstanding portfolio                      23          22           22
Principal outstanding for loans in arrears
over one year/outstanding portfolio-       56          54           49
As can be seen, the measures DBP has taken to remedt the situation have begun
to take effect and there has been a gradual but significant improvement in
the arrears on all industrial accounts.
6.         Appendices 10 and 11 summarize the arrears situation, and indicate
the magnitude of arrears between various types of industrial loans. As these
appendices show amounts in default on SMI loans are nearly three times higher
than for larger loans, while those for cottage industry loans are higher still.
These arrearages are too high; to a large extent they reflect the stringency
of DBP's own policies and practices with respect to: amortization schedules
which are generally too tight; collateral (para 11); and working capital (para 8)
which do not take account of the cash flow problems which confront smal-L
entrepreneurs. The weakness of DBP's follow-up has also contributed to these
high arrears. The appraisal mission discussed the arrears problem with DBP
and outlined various courses of action to reduce default rates through improve-
ments in arrearages accounting, loan follow-up and collection, and the setting
of more realistic maturity schedules. Although the arrears situation is improving
gradually, a major improvement will only be possible with the rescheduling of
existing debt along more realistic lines coupled with a well conceived and
executed program of loan follow-up (para. 21).
7.         Lending Priorities.  In making SMI loans DBP gives priority to SMI
outside the Greater Manila Areal/and outside the boundaries of Cebu and Davao
cities, and to export-oriented, labor-intensive, domestic-resource using aMI
in urban areas. This is in conformity with the Government's industrial
dispersal policy and with its SMI promotion program.  While the financing of
SMI in urban areas is not discouraged, especially for providing capital for
expansion of plant and inventory, urban SMI are not given the preferential
treatment (e.g. lower collateral requirements, higher loan/equity proportions
etc.) applicable to SMI in rural areas.
1/   Defined as the following area; on the north up to Malolos, Bulacan;
on the south down to Calamba, Laguna; on the east up to Angono and
Teresa, Rizal; and on the southwest down to Rosario and Cavite.



ANNEX 4
Page hT
8.        Use of DB?'s Loans.  loan proceeds can be used for the acquisition,
construction or improvement of fixed assets including land, buildings,
machinery and equipment, and for working capital.  The purposes for which
industrial loans have been approved in FY73 and FY74 are shown in Appendix
8.  No breakdown by size of loan is available.  In actutal practice DBP has
been restrictive in financing the working capital requirements of its
smaller borrowers and has tended, somewhat rigidly, to confine such financing
to cover 1-2 months working capital requirements. The mission feels that
DBP's financing of working capital requirements should be based on case-by-
case estimates of the requirements for a single complete production run.
Furthermore in view of the difficulty that small industry clients face in
dealing with several financial institutions, and the fact that most SMI
clients financed by DBP have nearly all their collateral mortgaged with
DBP (para. 11), the mission has recommended that DBP consider financing
working capital loans, separate from fixed asset financing, for existing
clients whose collateral would already cover the loan amount requested.
DBP should not, however, engage in making purely short-term trade accommo-
dations, but rather consider medium-term (2 - 3 years) loans which would
enable clients to increase their output on a sustained basis.
9.        Interest Rate and Maturity Period.  An interest rate of 12%,
the statutory maximum for secured loans, is charged on all SMI loans. DBP
presently allows maturity periods ranging from a minimum of 5 years to a
maximum of 10 years. On the basis of a review of recent SMI appraisal
reports, the mission feels that there is considerable room for more flexi-
bility in determining the maturity periods of SMI loans. Unduly heavy
emphasis is placed or. early repayment based on unrealistic cash generation
expectations which do not take sufficient account of growing working capital
needs as businesses develop (para. 6). More weight should be placed on the
productive life of financed assets in determining maturity periods. More
flexibility in this regard would also help to alleviate the relatively high
rate of arrearages for SMI loans and the frequency of account restructuring.
In addition to the interest rate, DBP also charges filing and processing
fees based on the size of the loan application. The schedule of fees is
shown in Appendix 9.
10.       Loan/fcuity Proportions.  D3P's policy requires that the borrower
put up a minimum equity contribution of 25% of total project cost
either in cash, in kind or in the form of collaterals. The mission feels
that a relaxation of the 75:25 ratio to 80:20 would make it considerably
easier for small entrepreneurs to avail of DBP financing. The 20%
equity contribution (together with existing collateral requirements)
still represents a substantial stake in -the project for small entrepreneurs.
11.       Collateral Requirements. DBP's policy requires that its SMI loans
are secured by a first mortgage on titled or untitled real estate, buildings,
machineries and equipment and other mortgageable assets either already owned
by the borrower or to be acquired with the proceeds of the loan. In the
case of corporations, the principal stockholders and officers may be required



ANNEX 4
Page 5
to sign as co-obligors. The maximum loan values allowed by DBP on various
types of collateral are as follows:
Collateral                              As % Appraised Value
a.  Titled land                                     90
b.  Untitled land                                   80
c.  Buildings and improvements
on titled land                                90
d.  Buildings and improvements
on untitled land                              80
e. Machinery and equipment                          80
On paper DBP's collateral requirements are more liberal than most term-
loan financial institutions, although many entrepreneurs still feel that DBP
is too collateral conscious. An analysis of recent SMI loans indicates
that loan amounts averaged about 50% of the appraised value of
collaterals accepted as security for the loan. No information is available
on the number of loan applications turned down, or loan amounts reduced,
because of insufficient collateral. As a first step the appraisal mission
has recommended that Branch and Departmental managers be urged to implement
DBP's collateral policies less conservatively. Performance should be
monitored by comparing loan amounts approved with the loan value of
collaterals accepted as security. The mission has also suggested that
a record be kept of applications turned down or loan amounts reduced
because of insufficient collateral, with a view to reviewing DBP's
collateral policies within the next year.
12.       Refinancing.  DBP's policy allows for the restructuring of its
own accounts where SMI clients have serious debt-servicing problems, but
not for the refinancing of obligations due to other institutions. In some
cases DBP also assists applicants in arranging short-term interim financing
with other financial institutions (particularly the Government-owned
Philippine National Bank) to cover their requirements while the DBP loan
is being processed.
13.       Contingencies and Cost Overrun Financing.  DBP does not at present
include any contingency allowances in its SMI loans, nor is it willing to
consider financing cost overruns due to increased prices. Many of DBP's
SMI clients feel that this is unfair since it is often DBP's long delays in
loan processing that contribute to their increased costs. Part of this
problem can be met by reducing DBP's long loan processing time (para. 15).
DBP should also make explicit allowances for price increases. These
measures notwithstanding, the mission believes that, in view of the very
high rates of inflation in the Philippines and elsewhere, there is still
a case for a policy permitting overrun financing up to 2% of total



ANNEX 4
Page 6
project cost with only a brief review of the project's continued viability.
It should also be part of DBP's decentralization measures to give Branch
and Departmental Managers the authority to approve overrun financing within
a certain ceiling, so that such financing could be made available with
minimum delay.
14.       Loan Processing Procedures.  The flow of present loan processing
procedures for loans above P 20,000 (i.e. above the Branch and IPD II        C
Managers' commitment authority) is depicted in Chart No. 9383. As can be
seen the procedures are needlessly complicated and there is much duplication
of work. Even worse, the process is extremely time-consuming. An analysis
by the mission of a random sample of recent &4I loan appraisals indicated
an average loan processing time (from payment of the application fee to
first release of funds) of 244 days for loans processed through the branches
and 196 days processed through IPD II. A separate study of IPD II's loan
processing procedures by the Development Academy of the Philippines (DAP)
in September 1974 showed that loan processing took about 100 days for loans
below P 20,000, and about 172 days for loans above p 20,000. Apart from
bureaucratic complexity and duplication of work, the following factors are
cited as additional causes of delay: -
(i)  The filing of incomplete loan aplications.  In many cases
collaterals are deficient, loan/equity requirements have
not been met, or financial statements are lacking. However,
much of the problem lies with DBP's loan examiners who
often accept applications (and application fees) which are
not complete. The problem is exacereated because
applicants are usually not fully informed about DBP's
lending policies, particularly DBP's method of appraisal
of collaterals which is very detailed and elaborate.
(ii)  Difficulty in contactng applicants, especially in the
Branches. The DAP study showed that frequent follow-up
by the applicant usually resulted in a faster processing
time.
(iii) Slow communications between Branches and Head Office.
During October 1974 DBP introduced a courier systEm
which has already improved the situation.



ANNEX 4
Page 7
(iv) lack of manpower and equipment. There are still many
unfilled positions in the Branches and IPD II, especially
in the operational divisions. Staff are hampered by the
shortage of vehicles and basic office equipment. DBP
has already accelerated its recruitment program
and is implementing an equipment replacement
program within its budgetary constraints.  This situation
has been aggravated by the substantial increase in the
number of loar. applications. The problem is not so acute
in Head Office where functional lines are clear-cut. In
the Branches, all loan applications, whether agricultural,
industrial or real estate, are handled by one division.
As of September 30, 1974 there were 18,627 loan applications
pending processing in the Branches (compared to 10 in I;PD II),
17,071 of which were agricultural loans, and 60 were industrial
loans above 100,000. Because agricultural loans are often given
greater priority, most of the Branches' scarce manpower -tends to
be allocated to agricultural lending.
(v) Delays in credit reports for loans above P 100,000.
Credit investigations are done by a separate department
(Credit Department). The DAP study found that in many
cases, credit investigation took longer than the project
evaluation.
15.       Recommendations.  In the mission's view, the loan processing problem
is the most serious one affecting DBP's SMI lending program, as reflected
in the criticism voiced both by SNI entrepreneurs and other government
agencies. The mission discussed this problem at length with DBP's management.
The conclusion was that it was both desirable and feasible to reduce DBP's
loan processing time to a maximum of 30 days for loans within the conmit-
ment authority of Branch Managers and the IPD II Manager, and 60 days for
loans that require the Chairman's or the Board's approval. The missicn
believes that the following measures need to be taken to achieve this target:
(i) Decentralization by increasing the commitment authority of
Branch Managers to P 200,000, and that of the IPD II Manager
to P500,000. The mission is strongly of the view that
these increases are essential and timely and would not
affect the quality of investment decisions made, or subject
DBP to unduly high risks.  Apart from the duplication involved,
the mission found that most Branch Managers are deeply resentful



ANNEX 4
Page o
of the present system under wlhich loans they have approved
are rechecked by relatively junior staff in Head Office. 1/
The present check should be replaced by a post-approval
review system in Head Office to monitor Branch Managers'
performarnce. The commitment authority limits should be
reviewed annually to adjust for inflation and for the
increased competence of Branch Managers and staff.
(ii) Procedural changes in the form of a simplification of
existing procedures and the elimination of unnecessary or
duplicated steps. The proposed changes are shown in
Chart Nos. 9384, 9385 and 9386.
(iii) One-man project appraisals of SMI projects by well-qualified
and thoroughly trained industrial appraisers at the Branches
and Head Office.  Credit investigation would be done
separately by a credit investigator, but within the same
department or division.
(iv) Streamlining of project appraisal reports.  Shorter and more
succinct appraisal reports need to be prepared. At present
reports tend to be rather long, descriptive and lacking in
analysis.
(v) Simplification of application and evaluation requirements.
DBP's application requirements for documents and project
information for SMI loans are unnecessarily complicated
and elaborate; if simplified they could considerably lighten
the appraisal load and make DBP financing more attractive
to SKI entrepreneurs. DBP has attempted to simplify appli-
cation requirements by introducing a questionnaire to replace
project feasibility studies for applications below P 500,000.
However, the questionnaire is extremely complex and requires
as much, if not more, information than the project feasibility
studies, and has met considerable resistance from entre-
preneurs, rJASIGAP teams and even DBP's own Branch staff.
DBP is presently revising the questionnaire to take into
account the comments of its Branches and the MkSICAP teams.
In addition DBP needs to provide more information and better
pre-filing counselling services to its clients, as well as
to screen applications more carefully, to avoid processirng
delays.
1/ An analysis of the rechecking done by the Head Office revealed that
very little was contributed by way of substantive judgements and that
investment decisions were never changed.



ANNEX 4
Page 9
(vi)  OrGanizational changes. Although DBP has recently comrpleted
some major organizational changes, both in Head Office and
in the Branches, the mission believes that the current
organization of each Branch, of IPD II and of BAAD can still
be considerably improved to utilize more efficiently ]3BP's
scarce specialized manpower resources (para. 16).
(vii)  Staffing Changes and Traning will be essential to support
recommendcations (i), (iii), and (iv) above.  These are
discussed in detail in paras. 17 and 18.
(viii) Improved Branch - Head Office communications. A systematic
review is needed to ensure that Branches are awrare of and
are imp lementing current DBP po'licies.  Prirt of this would
be covered urder the post-approval rev-.ew system mentioned
in (i) above. However, Branches sho-ld be made aware of
new policies as soon as they come into effect. The mission's
visits to 9 Branches indicated that Branches were rarely aware
of major policy changes for upto 3 - 4 months following their
introduction.
All the above recormendations were discussed at length with DBP's management.
DBP's management felt that a maximum processing time of 60 days was both
desirable and achievable. DBP has now adopted this objective as a target
to be reached as soon as possible. On the other recommendations made by the
mission DBP indicated that following internal discussions it would take them
up further during negotiations.  DBP has set up a Systemis Study Committee
chaired by the Manager of the Branches and Agencies Department, and has
h.-ed a consultant to carry out a stu&r and make recommendations.  T'he results
o3  the study have been sent to the Bank and have been found satis.factory.  DBP
has on the basis of this study instituted a program of action to achieve farther
red ct- 4 cs in loan prccessing time and more flexibi-l ity in its Lendtr;ng t-o S:4! I.
16.       Organization for &II Lending.  Taking full account of the problers
posed by DBP's disparate fields of activity, the appraisal mission has
suggested that DBP management consider organizing the Branches, IPD II and
BaAD along the lines shown in Chart Nos. 9382, 9377 and 9379. These proposals
entail some changes in the qualifications of staff required and the systems
and procedures currently being used.  They would not result in any overall
increases in staff requirements; in some instances they would result in
reductions.  The principal changes suggested are:
('I  Bra-cieS:.  ep.ara`ion of agrict;urai iendain  operati Olls frorn
SMI, real estate and other operations; combinataon of appraisal
anrd fc-llow--ap functions; and separation of very small (under
F5,i'00  for agriculture and un-der ai20,,C0O  for others) loans from
a, er ' oans in each of the two broad divis sions (Chart No. 938) .
(ii)  IPD II:  two broad divisions - project evaluat,ion and proJect
sunervision, with each of these divisions having one section
dealing with loans under P 20,000 and 4 - 5 other sections
differentiated along industry lines (Chart No. 9377).



ANN EX 4
Page 10
(iii) Branches and Agencies Department. two broad divisions -
performance audit and budget analysis (Chart No. 9379).
17.       Staffing.  Staffing changes are essential to support the
recommendations in paras. 15 - 16'. These include:
(i) Branches: at least 1 - 2 engineers with training in
financial and marketing analysis to concentrate solely
in SMI lending and follow-up in the smaller Branches and
3 - 4 such staff in the larger Branches. These could be
substituted for existing positions, such as loan examiners,
which would no longer be needed under the proposed loan
processing procedures.
(ii) IPD II: less specialized, broadly qualified project
evaluators for both appraisal and follow-up work to replace
the six different categories of specialized staff.
(iii) Branches and Agencies Department (BAAD): generalist
Branch performance auditors (one for every two Branches)
with practical experience in Branch operations and
trained to appraise all aspects of Branch operations.
They would, on occasion need to borrow specialists from
DBP's operating departments to look into specific
technical problems. These would replace the five cate-
gories of staff presently in the Branch Management and
Evaluation Division of the BAAD.
18.       Training.  Essential programs in support of the above reccommenda-
tions would include: -
(i)  Project evaluation for SNI projects:  an intensive 3 - 4 months
course for Branch and IPD II appraisal staff, including
classroom training in technical, financial and marketing
analysis and on-the-job training at Head Office. A less in-
tensive 2 - 3 week course could be held for Branch Managers,
Assistance INanagers and operational division chiefs. Priority
in training should be given to staff in Branches with large
industrial portfolios.
(ii)  Branch performance auditing:  a 2 - 3 month'training program
in Branch operations evaluation, systems and procedures and
staffing practices for a select group of staff to be located
in BAAD.
(iii)  Planning and budgeting for Branch operations:  a 4-seek
program, based on a well prepared budget manual, for Branch
Managers, Assistant Managers, and Branch accountants.
19.       Project Appraisal:  While the quality of DBP's S5I loan appraisals
are generally adequate as a basis for sound investment decisions, appraisal
reports tend to be unduly elaborate, lengthy and descriptive. This is due



AfNNEX ti
Page 11
in large part to the bureaucratic nature of the loan processing procedures
and a conservative approach towards collateralization.  At present separate
technical, market and credit reports are prepared plus an elaborate and
well-documented evaluation of the collateral presented.  A financial
analyst then prepares his report and a recommendation based on the other
reports. After this the recommendation goes through an elaborate review
process (see paras. 14 - 15 and Chart No. 9383). In the mission's view,
the technical and market reports and the recomnendation can be done by an
industrial appraiser. The credit investigation can be more quickly and
efficiently carried out by a credit investigator.  The mission believes
that this one-man appraisal system, with the right kind of organizat"ional
and staffing changes and training (paras. 16 - 18), is the most appropriate
one for the appraisal of &C loans, and will result in shorter and more
effective appraisals.  Appraisals  will continue to provide a sound basis
for investment decisions, while DBP's scarce manpower resources will be
more efficiently utilized.  DBP?s management has studied and accepted this
suggestion.
23.       Procurement and Disbursement.  DBP's procedures for onrocuremenit
and disbursement under its SMI loans as currently practiced are adequate.
While competitive bidding is not required, DBP's staff make a careful check
of the price and suitability of the assets to be acquired, both at the
appraisal stage, and again when the loan is being released.  Loans are
disbursed against supporting documents and after an on-site inspection by
DBP's staff of the assets to be acquired or the services to be rendered
with the proceeds of the loan.
21.       Loan Follow-up.  D3P has adequate Drocedures for loan collection,
servicing and foreclosure.  Penalty charges on past due loan accounts are
shown in Appendix 9.  HowevPer,DBP's management has acloowlsdged that its
follow-up work on SII loans is weak.  On paper, reporting requirements
from clients seem adequate, but the required statements and information
are not regularly received, and DBP only makes an effort to enforce these
requirements  hnen a client is badly in arrears.  When inforilation is
received it is usually of poor quality, and statements are not systermatically
reviewed, again unless the client is seriously in arrears. DBP cites the
shortage of manpower as the main reason for the lack of systenatic follow-up.
In view of the importance of follow-up work in improving the quality of
DBP's SMI loan portfolio, the mission reco:nrends that the following measures
be taken:
(i)  Use of collection agencies:  Very small industrial loar
accounts (below P 20,000), accounting for 1,796 outstanding
industrial loan accounts in Head Office and in the Branches
which are badly overdue (over 6 months) should be sub-
contracted to outside collection agencies.  At the moim<ent
DBP is using a disproportionate amount of manpower to
supervise these accounts.



ANNY
Page 12
(ii)  Combination of ap=raisal and follow-up functions in the
Branches. While recognizing that philosophies vary on
the separation of appraisal and follow-up, the mission
feels that such separation in the Branches is inefficient
and ineffective.  From discussions at the Branches and
Head Office, it seems that the principal reason for
separation was a basic lack of trust in the appraiser's
honesty and capability to acknowledge previous errors.
The mission does not feel this is adequate justification
for maintaining as inefficient a system as DBP presently
has. Combining appraisal and follow-up for SMI projects
would have the advantages of flexibility in staff use and
continuity in appraiser-client relationships.
(iii) Stricter enforcement of present supervision requi eaents.
Specifically, the following should represent the basic
information requirements:       -
(a)  Loans between P 20,000 - 100,000:  annual income
statements and balance sheets in a simplified
standard fcrmat.
(b)  Loans P 100,001 - 1,000,000:  semi-annual income
statements and balance sheets, annual sources
and application of funds statement and projections
for following two years; annual financial state-
ments should be audited; minimum of one project
site inspection per year or as frequently as
necessary for delinquent accounts.
(c)  Loans P 1,000,001 - 2,500,000:  same as in kb)
above except income statements and balance sheets
should be submitted on a quarterly basis.
(iv)  Systematic Review:  Staff at Head Office and the Branches
should systematically review submitted statements and comment
on their quality, record their findings in summary form on
project follow-up record, submit standard summary project
inspection reports, and, if necessary, make recommendations
for future action. These reports can form the basis of DBP's
reports to IBRD oia the progress of subprojects.
22.       Planning and Budgeting. At present IPD II and the Branches do not
undertake any systematic planning of their 314I operations. No attempt has
been made to match the future workload in the various categories of activity
with budgetary requirements. The assumption has been that the amount of
work will always exceed the available manpower. Applications for loars have
generally been accepted without any thought being given as to the availability
of manpower to process them. The result has been a massive backlog of loans
pending processing. The mission suggests that DBP should introduce a planni-g
and budgeting system so that it can manage its S3I operations on a less
haphazard basis. She planning and budgeting system could be relatively



ANNEX L
Page 13
simple, ancd would essentially be based on a forecast of SMI activities
(promotion, process-ng and evaluation, collection and follow-up)
over a two-year period and the budgeting requirements to carry out these
activities. This proposal complements the mission's recommendations on
organizational changes (para. l5). TWork on a planning and budgeting
system for the Branches, where the problems caused by the lack of
planning are particularly acute, has already begun. This work should
be extended and refined.  Complete budgets which include lending and
collection forecasts for various types of loans and cover all categories
of cost elements should be prepared and submitted to BAAD about three
months before the beginning of each fiscal year. The budgets should be
reviewed by a committee chaired by the Manager, BAAD and including
managers from the operating departments, before submission to the
Chairman's office for approval. Once approved all authority for expendi-
tures within the approved budgets should be given to the Branch Manager.
For expenditures exceeding the approved budget, approval of the Manager,
BAAD should be sought if the amount is up to P 50,000 and of the Chairmnan
for larger amounts. Monthly reports on variances from budgets should
be prepared for  review by the Budget Division of the BAAD so that Branch
perfonmance in meeting lending and other targets controlling costs can
be regularly monitored. These proposals were discussed at length with
DBP's management who indicated that the question of planning and budgeting
would be addressed as part of the systems study mentioned in para. 15.
23.       Coordination with the Department of Industry and Commission
on Small and Medium Industries. DBP is presently represented on the
Commission on anall and Medium Industries (CSMI) by the Manager of the
Branches and Agencies Department with the Manager of IPD II as alternate.i/
The DBP representative on the CS g is also the Chairman of the Finance
Sub-Committee of the CSMC. The Managers of BAAD and IPD II also hold
regular meetings with the National Coordinatior of MASICAP to discuss and
resolve problems related to MASICAP projects referred to DBP for financing.
At the field level, relations betwen DBP Branch staff and MASICAP teams
range from cooperative to strained. M1uch depends on the personalities
involved.  However, most DBP Branch Managers recognize that MASICAP teams
can and do play a useful role and in many cases refer potential proponents
to MASICAP for assistance in project preparation. The MASICAP teams for
their part are beginning to understand the constraints under which DBP
Branches operate. The mission considers it essential for the success of
tbhe SMI program that close cooperative relationships be maintained between
DBP and MASICAP, at both Branch and Head Office levels.
lJ DBP has since accepted the mission's recommendation to reverse this
order. The Manager, IPD II is now the full-time representative iwhile
the Manager, BAAD serves as alternate.



ANNEXES 4
Page 14
B. Features of the Proposed Ican
24.       ProJect DescriDtion.  The DBP portion of the loan (US$ 15 million)
would be used to finance part of its projected SN loan commitments during
FY76 and FY77. The maximum size of sub-loan under the proposed loan would
be about US$360,000, and the average size is expected to be about US$t5,000.
Although no minimum loan size will be set, in practice the minimum would
probably be about P 50,000 (US$7,000) for the reasons outlined in para. 2.
This limitation is based onloan size for administrative simplicity. The
main consideration in lending is    to reach the intended 511C target group,
defined as enterprises with total fixed assets of between P 100,000 and
P 24,000,000. Within this definition eligible subprojects should include
as wide a range as possible of projects in manufacturing, agro-industry,
tourism, transportation services, engineering repair facilities of various
types and small construction industries (with the exceptions outlined in
para. 26). No discrimination either by industry branch or by location
would be specified under the loan. Discrimination by location seems
unnecessary in view of the Government's expressed policy of investment
dispersal away from Greater Minila, Cebu and parts of Davao, and DBP's
affirmation that it will conform to the Goverment's policies in implementing
its S4I program. Both fixed assets and permanent working capital would be
financed by the proceeds of the proposed loan.  Loans over P1 million woua
be handled by IPD I and would absorb upto a maximum of US$5 million (P35
million)'.
25.       Amount of Loan. As indicated in para. 4, DBP is projected to make
531 SMI loans in FY76 and FY77 amounting to P208.6 million (US$29.8 million).
Assuming 75% coverage of DBP's loans over this period (para. 28), the
size of the IBRD loan to cover fully the two-year period would need to be P156.5
million (US$22.4 million). HowTever, in view of the uncertainties regarding the
effects of increased competition from IGLF, the ability of DBP to improve its
SMI loan processing capacity, and the .investment outlook for the Philippine
industrial sector as a whole resulting from the interrnational economic situation,
it is considered prudent to allow for some overestimation and limit the DBP
portion to only US$15 million at this stage with close monitoring of its rate of
utilization.
26.       Overlap with other IBRD loans through DBP. As indicated in para.24,
the proposed loan would be used to finance as wide a range of S14I subprojects
as possible. However, the proposed DBP component of US$15 million would not
be used to finance projects already eligible for financing under IBRD's
existing or future loans for grain processing, fisheries, livestock, and
shipping. Decisions regarding possible overlaps with IBRD's existing and
future loans through DBP for industrial investment oould be left to the
discretion of DBP?s management. The Bank expects DBP to follow the principle
that sub-borrowers eligible under both the proposed SMI loan and the IBRD
loan for industrial investment will be financed by the latter if the
application is for the financing of direct imports, and by the former if
the application is for domestically procured goods and services. However,
the numlber of cases which might fall into this category is not expected
to exceed ten at the maximum.



ANNEX 4
Page 15
27.         Form of Bank Lending.  The MBP component of the proposed a4I loan
would be made to the Government which would relend the proceeds to DB:P on the
same terms as that of the IBRD loan to the Government.
28.       Proportion of Sub-loans to be Covered by Proceeds of IBRD
Loan. Since small entrepreneurs rarely engage in the direct importation
of capital goods or raw material requirement, sub-loans would be made
entirely in local currency. On the basis of a detailed analysis prepared
by DBP, the foreign exchange component (direct and indirect) of fixed
asset costs for typical Philippine aiI projects was estimated to be about
64% of total sub-project cost (Annex 10). It is expected that on
average DBP financing will cover about 80 percent of total subproject
costs including working capital. For administrative simplicity, 75%
of the loan amounts advanced by DBP would be covered by the proceeds of
the proposed loan.
29.       Foreign Exchange Risk.  The foreign exchange risk on the loan
would be borne by the Government as in the case of the livestock and
fisheries loans.
30.         Procurement.  The Bank's normal requirements for international
competitive bidding would be clearly inappropriate for application to
subprojects under this loan as in standard Bank loans to development f'inance
companies. As indicated in para. 20, DBP has adequate procedures to ensure
that purchases from the proceeds of its sub-loans are made with due regard
for economr and efficiency. Through routine supervision missions the Bank
can ascertain that these procedures are consistently and effectively applied.
31.         Amortization Schedule.  A fixed amortization schedule of 16 years
including a 4-year grace period is based on the assumption that sub-loans
would have a maximum repayment period of 12 years including a 2-year period
of grace, and that the funds would be fully committed within two years, and
fully disbursed within three years, of the date of loan signature.
32.         Relending Rate.  DBP would charge an interest rate of 12% per
annum to sub-borrowers. This is the maximum legal rate applicable to secured
loans in the Philippines. If DBP is allowed to increase its lending rate,
the Bank would be consulted on the onlending rate to be charged to sub-
borrowers under the proposed loan.



ANNEX h
Page 16
33.       Free Limit.  A total of about 360 sub-loans is expected to be
made by DBP out of the proceeds of this loan.  In this instance the Bank
would dispense with free limits and prior approvals for the following
reasons:
(i) DBP's ability to appraise and evaluate SMI projects is
aalready adequate, and prior approval of sub-loans by the
Bank would neither be an efficient nor an appropriate
mechanism for improving DBP's SMI appraisals. Nor would
prior approval by the Bank help DBP in avoiding bad loans
since judgements regarding the feasibility and viability
of SMI sub-loans must to a large extent depend on highly
localized factors. The Bank would not be in a position
to second-guess DBP in this regard.
(ii)  The Bank has already set a free limit of US$1 million
(compared with a maximum sub-loan size of US$360,000 under
the proposed loan) for DBP under the previous industrial
investment loan (Loan No. 998-PH).
(iii)  One of the main objectives of the proposed loan is to speed
up DBP's loan processing time for SMI subprojects. Prior
approval by the Bank would contradict this objective by
increasing DBP's target of 60 days loan processing time
by at least 50 percent.
Instead a system of periodic post-reviews, based on appropriately designed
statements of approvals submitted by DSP, together with an examination
of appraisal reports for all sub-loan approvals above P500,000 (U$7o,ooo)
would be instituted. This coupled with more frequent project review
missions, at least in the initial stages of project implementation, would
suffice to ensure that sub-loan appraisals are based on sound financial,
economic . technical and market considerations.
34.       Disbursements.  Loan disbursements would be made on a reirmbursement
basis against quarterly statements provided by DBP, showing amounts withdrai
against the individual subprojects approved.
EAP Projects Department

PHILIPPINES
DEVELOPMENT BANK OF THE PHILIPPINES
Schedule of Fees and Charges
A. Filing and Processing Fees
Loan Amount A-plied For                          Fee
P 2,000 and below                              P30
P 2,001 - 10,000                               P 80
P 10,001 - 20,000                             p 100
P 20,001 - 30,000                               150
P 30,001 - 40,000                               250
P 40,001 - 50,000                               350
P 50,001 - 100,000                              500
P 100,001 - 500,000                           1 ,000
P 500,001 - 1,000,000                         2;500
Over P 1,000,OOC                              1,000 for every million pesos
applied for with a minimum of
P 5,000.
B. Penalty Changes
1. Loan amortizations or portions thereof which have been past due for more
than ninety (90) days shall be subjected to the penalty charge of 1/3% per
month counted from the date they become liable to such charge. The term
amortization shall be construed to mean any periodic amount due and
payable at specific dates, whether fixed or changing in amount, and
irrespective of whether it is composed of both interest and principal
repayment or exclusively of either one. Where applicable, loans which
are exempted from the increased interest rate of 12% per annum shall be
subject to this penalty charge.
2.  Since the penalty charge of 1/3% per month cannot be imposed unilaterally
by the Bank on existing accounts in the absence of a condition in the
loan or mortgage contract, all transactions involving novation of existing
loan agreements enumerated elsewhere in this recommendation, as well as
approvals of additional, liquidation or new credit accommodations shall
be subject to the 1/3% per month penalty charge.
3. In lieu of the foregoing penalty charge provided above, where an account
is re-structured, i.e., terms of repayment are re-scheduled for the
benefit of the borrower, in order to drop foreclosure action initiated
by the Bank, failure on the part of the borrower to comply with the
terms of the restructuring agreement shall subject the borrower to a
penalty charge of 10% per annum to be levied on total amortizations past
due, effective thirty (30) days after the violation is committed. Th-Ls
penalty shall be charged for as long as tlae violation of the restructuring
agreement remains uncorrected and the mortgage is not foreclosed.
4. If an account is endorsed for legal action and foreclosure is actually
accomplished, the usual 10% attorney's fees on the total obligation shall
be imposed.
EAP Projects Department
December 5, 1974

ANNEX 5

THE INDUSTRIAL GUARANTEE AND LOAN FUND (IG.LF)
Backgroimn d
1.        The IGLF was established in 1952 under Counterpart Project
Agreement No. 32 (CPA 32) between the Philippine Council for United
States Aid l/and the Economic Co-operatior. Administration2/of the US.
CPA 32 was revised on January 25, 1965 and again on July 16, 1973. IGLF
was originally set up as a revolving fund to provide both compensatory
financing for, and to guarantee lending by "qualified banksf"f/to small
and medium sized industrial, mining and other enterprises. Its primary
purpose has remained unchanged through successive revisions to CPA 32
although its focus is now exclusively on small-scale industrial and agro-
industrial enterprises. As it presently stands, the IGLF is a fund (not
an institution) owned by NEDA and administered by the Central Bank (CB)
under the terms of a Master Agreement4/between them.  The operations of
IGLF are guided by formally agreed upon "rules and regulationsll-/which
are an integral part of CPA 32.
6/
Main Features of CPA 32-
2.        Under the terms of CPA 32 the IGLF is to be used specifically
for providing:
(i)  Special Time Deposits (STDs):  to sponsoring banks equi-
valent to the amount of their loan for a small industry
project, for a period of time equivalent to the maturity
period. The present STD rate is 5% and the on-lending
rate is limited to 10%.
1/   Predecessor to the National Economic Council, which later became the
National Economic Development Authority (NEDA).
2/   Predecessor to USAID.
3/ These include: commercial, investment, savings, r-ural and development
banks and finance companies which are subject, to the Central Bank's
regulations under its Charter.
4/   The original agreement between the CB and PHILCUSA dated July 15, 1952
was replaced by a new agreement between the CB and NEC(later NEDA)
dated January 22, 1965 wherein CB accepted its re-appointment as
administrator for a 10-year period. The agreement was renewed on
January 22, 1975.
5/   These were last revised at the same time as CPA 32, on July 16, 1973.
~/   As last amended.



ANNEX 5
Paage Z
(ii)  Guarantees:  Up to a maximum of 80% of the amount of the
loan provided by a sponsoring bank. A guarantee fee of
2% of the amount guaranteed is charged.2/  Total guarantee
obligations are not to exceed 75% of IGLF's total assets.
The lending rate for guaranteed loans is 12%.
(iii) Combined STD/Guarantees: Under the same terms and con-
ditions as described above, the interest rate charged to
sub-borrowers under the combined arrangement is 12%.
3.        These financing mechanisms were devised to address the problems
of:
(i)  financial intermediaries:  who traditionally have not had
access to long-term funds at a rate sufficiently attractive
for them to engage in lending to small industry, a credil;
segment traditionally perceived as involving high-costs and
high risks; and
(ii)  small entrepreneurs:  with insufficient collateral to obtain
financing on conventional commercial terms.
4.        Project Selection Criteria:  CPA 32 also specified that the
following priorities were to be applied in project selection for IGLF
financing:
(i)  compatibility with current 4-year development plan
priorities and with those of the Board of Investments
(BOI);
(ii)  compatibility with the policy decisions of the Investment
Co-ordinating Committee (ICC) of NEDA;
(iii)  labor intensity;
(iv) export-orientation;
(v)  location away from major urban centers.
5.        Furthermore, special emphasis was placed on providing more
guarantees to compensate for collateral deficiencies and on the provision
of working capital finance. The terms and conditions of IGLF funding
were to be subject to the final approval of the Director-teneral of NEDA
with the following overall limitations on:
1/   The fee was originally paid by the sponsoring bank but the IGLF Review
Committee passed a resolution in early 1974 permitting this fee to be
passed on to the sub-borrower.



ATNEX 5
Page 3
(i)  Interest Rates:  the rates charged to sponsoring baxks
could not exceed by one per cent prevailing rates on
similar deposits of their clients; and on-lending rates
charged by sponsoring banks had to be comparable to, and
in no case in excess of rates currently charged on medium
and long-term loans.
(ii) Maturity and Grace Periods: no limits were specified on
maturity periods (which presently cannot exceed 10 years
for fixed asset loans and 3 years for working capital loans);
STD loans were generally to have a grace period of 6 months
but in no instance could grace periods be longer than a quarter o.
the total maturity period; and prepayments of loans made
under IGLF automatically required prepVyments of STDs.
(iii) Disbursement Periods: the full amount of the loan had to
be disbursed within one year of approval or during the
construction/installation period (if this is longer).
Balances undisbursed after that period were not eligible
for STD coverage.
(iv) Loan Ceilings: the loan ceiling for which IGLF STD's or
guarantees would be provided was set at P500,000 with a
proviso for flexibility in individual cases. (However,
in view of a shortage of resources IGLF has applied this
ceiling rigidly).
Mechanics of IGLF Operation and its Administration
6.        The mechanics of the IGLF approval process are depicted in
Appendix 1. Project appraisal and general administration is undertaken
by the CB's Department of Loans and Credits (DLC). Project appraisal
review and final recommendations for approval of IGLF facilities are
the responsibility of a representatively constituted Review Committee.
The Committee is chaired by the Director, Industries and Public Utilities
Division, NEDA (or in his absence by the Asst. Director), and includes
senior level representatives from the Institute for Small Scale Industries
(ISSI), Board of Investments/Department of Industry (BOI/DDI), and the
Central Bank. While CPA 32 relegates the Review Committee to an evaluation
and advisory role, and vests executive power only in the Director-General
of NEDA,1/ the Review Committee through its Chairman has, in practice, been
the final decision-making authority on individual project applications.
7.        Until recently, IGLF's procedures as specified under CPA 32 were
extraordinarily cumbersome and often took as long as 6-? months between the
submission of an application to CB by the sponsoring bank and its eventual
approval. In March 1974 procedures were further rationalized with sponsor-
ing banks submitting applications directly to the CB for appraisal without
1/   Under CPA 32, the authority for approval rests solely with the Director-
General of NEDA who has now delegated it to the Director, Industries and
Public Utilities Division.



ANNEX 5
Page 4
the prior approval of the Review Committee. As a result processing time
was reduced to a maximum of 3-4 weeks.
Management and Staffing of the IDLF Supervisory Unit at CB
8.        The DLC is a well-managed department run by a competent, dynamic
Director. Within the department the Industrial Loans Division (ILD) deals
solely with IGIF operations. It has a staff of 18 (with 16 professionals)
who have a reasonable mix of educational and professional qualifications.L/
Virtually all staff have long working experience and are generally well
qualified for loan appraisal and examination work. Current staff strengrth
reflects a considerable expansion over staff levels in June 1973, when
ILD had 5 professional staff.  With the current inflow of operations, the
ILD is likely to become a bottleneck in processing operations unless the
focus of staff effort at the CB is changed radically (as recommended in
paragraph 31) or staff strength augmented substantially.
Project Appraisal and Follow-up
9.        ILD's appraisal of project applications submitted by sponsoring
banks is brief but not inappropriately so relative to the type of project
being appraised, the sophistication of the entrepreneur, and the quality
of information generally available. Until recently appraisals, at least
as reflected in ILD's reports, tended to be descriptive rather than
analytic and not sufficiently incisive about potential problems which
projects might encounter. Since the pre-appraisal mission's visit in
January 1974, their quality has improved.
10.       Rather than suffering from basic weaknesses in appraisal metho-
dology or capability, ILD's appraisals reflect more the difficulty with
extracting accurate and reliable information from applicants and the
inability to verify information against other sources. For IGLF
financed projects, sponsoring banks are required to assist applicants
with completing a standard and simple application form. This, along with
information about the applicant and the sponsoring bank, includes:
essential information on the technique of production to be employed and
output; projected sales; marketing arrangements and markets to be served;
and financial statements projected over a 3-year period.  Although relatively
simple when compared to information requirements for a standard IFC financed
project, most small entrepreneurs have difficulty in supplying even this
1/   All professional staff have at least a Bachelor's degree.  Most have
membership in a professional body. ILD's staff include 4 CPA's,
4 Commerce graduates, 3 industrial engineers, 3 law graduates and
2 staff with business administration backgrounds. Appraisal staff
have had either formal or on-the-job training in industrial project
evaluation.



ANNEX 5
Page 5
basic data without assistance either from their bookkeepers or more often
from "consultants" who charge up to 3% of the loan amount for their
services. Information on the application form is often (but not always)
augmented by a brief (1-2 day) field visit, by the CB appraiser who inter-
views both the applicant (and conducts a site inspection) and the sponsoring
bank, following which an appraisal report is prepared.
11.       In a few instances the CB appraisers' judgements and final
recommendations were evaluated against on-the-spot information from
project proponents and sponsoring banks. These suggested that the brief
second-hand nature of the appraisal often reflected a lack of appreciation
for local conditions and adversely influenced the quality of the ultimate
judgements made. Appraisers, restricted to a very brief visit, also tended
to be somewhat abrupt and unsympathetic toward applicants, with little
opportunity for the normal transfer of advice and assistance which accompanies
the appraisal process. Both CB staff, the sponsoring banks and the few
entrepreneurs interviewed expressed the unanimous view that the quality of
appraisal would be considerably better and the efficiency of the appraisal
process improved if appraisal was conducted as close to the local level as
possible, preferably by sponsoring banks.
12.        Once projects are approved and facilities provided by the CB,
virtually no sustained follow-up work is carried out by the ILD. Follow-
up by sponsoring banks varies widely in intensity and quality. At present
few sponsoring banks keep track of their SKI clients on a systematic basis;
most look into client performance only when accounts fall in arrears.  Sponsor-
ing banks and the CB generally agree that follow-up is weak and that steps
need to be taken to strengthen such capability in sponsoring banks -at the branch
level.
Operations
13.        IGLF's operations are summarized in Appendix 2.  In the 22-year
period since its inception IGLF has approved financial assistance totalling
nearly P193 million for 375 projects. As the data in Table 1 suggest,
IGLF's operations underwent a radically different orientation following
its restructuring in July 1973.
14.        Special Time Deposit (STD) Operations.  Total STD approvals
(including those with accompanying guarantees) since 1952 amount to
nearly P177.3 million or about 92% of total IGLF assistance approved.



ANNEX S
Page 6
Of this ar2Dunt roughly P153.8 million had been approved prior to a
restructuring of the IGLF in July 1973.  Between 1952-1965 approvals
averaged roughly P10-12 million annually.  They fell off sharply to aa
annual level of P2-3 million between 1965-72. In FY72-73 total approvals
amounted to P1.63 million for 4 projects.  The sharp decline in the level
of operations since 1965 was mainly due to the cumbersome administratiLve
procedures governing the approval and release of IGLF facilities coupled
with an inadequate margin between STD interest rates and on-lending rates
that sponsoring banks were permitted to charge. By 1973 most sponsoring
banks had virtually ceased using IGLF facilities. Although fairly drastic
measures were taken in July 1973 to streamline IGLF's administrative
procedures and reduce processing time, sponsoring banks did not overcome
their skepticism easily. Only 8 projects amounting to R325,000 were
submitted by 3 sponsoring banks batTeeri JTUly l-December 31, 1973. Following
an intensive promotional campaign by NEDA and the CB,applications for IGLF
assistance began picking up in March 1974 with approvals rising from 4z
projects totalling P1.8 million to 38 projects totalling over F7.5 mi]Llion
in October 1974. (Chart No. 9391).
15.       Size of STD.  Although originally intended as a financing
vehicle to encourage the dispersal of modern small factory units away from
Manila, IGLF operations between 1952-73 tended to favor the medium-sized
Manila based enterprise.  The average size of STD approved durnn  this
period was nearly P660,000 for all projects and about P766,0001/for pro-
jects in the Manila area. After July 1973 the average size STD's dropped
to P182,000 although Manila-based projects continue to be considerably
larger than those located elsewhere. The reduction in average size
reflects an exclusive focus on small enterprises and the limitation of'
IGLF financing to a maximum of f5O0,OOO.  The recent inflow of applications
suggests that STD's will continue to range from the very small (P10,000) to
the fairly sizeable (P500,000) with a larger cluster at the latter end.
Average STD size over the commitment period for the proposed loan is likely
to be around P200,000.
16.       Industrial Distribution of STD's.  Prior to 1973 IGLF financing
was provided for a broad range of industries although nearly 58% of total
STI approvals were concentrated in five industries (food processing;
textiles; wood and wood products; metal products and non-metallic minerals).
Since 1973 however the range of industries to which IGLF resources have
been directed has narrowed to include only those in which small enterprises
predominate. In FY73-74, 68.3% of the STD's approved were confined to the
apparel and footwear, wood and wood products, and metal, products industries.
1/   In equivalent terms these average amounts, most of which were approved
between 1952-1965, suggest far larger projects than could be supported
with the same amounts in 1974 pesos.



ANNEX 5
Page 7
17.       Geographic Distribution.  The data in Appendix 2 indicate that
since IGLF's restructuring the objective of dispersing investment away
from the Greater Manila areas is being gradually achieved. Before 1973
over 67% of the number STD's approved and nearly 79% of the amount were
for projects located in the Greater Manila area.   In FY73-74 these
proportions were reduced to 32.4% by number and 57.4% by amount respecti-
vely, approvals in the first four months of FY74-75 show an even lower
proportion by amount (48.6%) for Greater Manila. Although investment in
FY73-74 is being directed away from Manila, the bulk of it is still
concentrated in the island of Luzon. FY74-75 approvals to date and the
projects in the pipeline provide encouraging signs that this imbalance
will gradually be corrected with the Visayas and Mindanao likely to account
for increasing proportions.
18.       Guarantee Operations.  As Appendix 2 shows IGLF's straight
guarantee operations (i.e. independent of STD's) have been at a standstill
for sometime. Only 13 guarantees averaging over P1.2 million in size have
been approved in the entire history of IGLF, despite the importance
attached to its guarantee function in its original "charter"1. The low
level of straight guarantee operations has been the result of CB's basic
conservatism coupled with reluctance on the part of sponsoring banks to
pay a fee for guarantee cover which was generally not provided until due
processes of foreclosure and ensuring litigation were completed. The time,
and administrative costs incurred in such instances deterred intermediary
banks from using the guarantee facility.  While the role of straight
guarantees has been re-emphasized in the amended version of CPA 32, it
seems unlikely that any sponsoring banks assisting SKI will apply for
straight guarantees only. These institutions do not confront the problem
of having sufficient long-term resources but being unwilling or unable to
lend them on account of insufficient security. Their basic problem is
access to long-term resources at a sufficiently attractive rate to enable
them to lend long-term.
19.       Although IGLF's straight guarantee operations seem unlikely to
develop, its combined STD/Guarantee operations have increased substantially.
Between 1952-1973 only 20 STD/G's had been approved totalling P12.5 million
on which the total guarantee cover provided was P8.2 million, thus account-
ing for about 8.5% of total STD approvals by number and 8.1% by amount
over the period. In FY73-74, however, combined STD/G's amounting to
P1.1   million for 13 projects were approved accounting for 42% by number
and 19.4% by amount. For the first four months of FY74-75 the proportion
of STD/G's was even higher, 51.5% by number and 59.9% by amount. Since
the combined STD/G addresses both the resource problem of the sponsoring
bank and the collateral problem of the small entrepreneur, its prominence
in the share of total IGLF financing is likely to increase. As recommended
in paragraph 34 it would make the most sense to provide all IGLF financing
in the form of combined STD/G's.



ANNEX 5
Page 8
20.        Pipeline of Operations.  As the upsurge in applications to the
IGLF is a relatively recent phenomenon, the historical record of per-
formance is a poor guide in determining the level of approvals that is
likely to be sustained over the next 2 years. Since July 1974, applications
totalling between F4-6 million have been received every month.  As of
January 31, 1974 applications totalling  l14.0 million were being processed
by ILD.  Total resources available for commitment amounted to P264,000 million
as of December 31, 1974.  Taking into account likely growth in levels of SMI
investment, coupled with increases in financial assistance being provided by
traditional sources of finance (i.e. DBP), IGLF resource requirements up to
December 31, 1976 are projected at between F110-120 million (Appendix 3).

between them.

23.        IGLF ran out of its own resources in January 1975. GOP has
provided IGIF with the necessary resources on appropriate terms to ensure
the continuity of its operations until the proposed Bank loan becomes
effective. GOP will also be expected to provide a further counterpart
contribution of P30 million to IGLF's nequity" to match the proposed Bank
loan of US$12 million in the ratio of 1:3.
24.       Apart from executing the project appraisal and general administ-
ration functions for IGLF, the CB is also responsible for management of its
finances.17 IGLF assets comprise cash balances held with CB, outstanding
STD's, and its investment portfolio. With the virtual cessation of its
lending operations in 1973, IGLF had built up substantial cash balances with
the CB together with a sizeable investment portfolio in short and medium
term deposits. However with the recent upsurge in its operations. there has
been a significant shift in its asset structure as shown below:

25.       In recent years income from IGLF's investment portfolio has
roughly equalled its income from STD and guarantee operations. This is
expected to change in future years as IGLF's funds switch out of temporary
investments into STD's. Income is also likely to be lower as the yield on
STD's is expected to be lower than the yield received on short and medium
term deposits with the CB. Gross income in FY74 totalled almost F2.2
million. With interest expenses of FO.95 million and the deduction of a
fee for administrative costs (paragraph 26) by CB of F0.34 million, net
income for the year was FO.8 million all of which was plowed back into
IGLF capital.

the Review Committee on the basis of "fgood fund-management practice"r
and subject to the review of the Director-General, NEDA.
