Administrative Costs
26.       F. ilts services in administering the IGLF's operations and
managing its finances, CB is "authorized to withdraw from IGLF (with
NEDA approval) a sum not exceeding 0.25% of the highest outstanding
level of STD's and guarantees during the period (usually the fiscal
year) immediately preceding". In the next few years if the current
level of IGLF operations is sustained CB's administrative expenses are
likely to run at an amount substantially higher than will be covered by
the allowable 0.25% of peak outstandings, until a larger
portfolio is built up to a stable level.  It may therefore be essential
to either: (i) reconsider the 0.25% limit to be charged against IGLF
earnings (ii) have a direct cost coverage (or cost-sharing) agreement
between CB and NEDA to be provided through their respective budgets without
charging administrative costs against IGLF earnings. The terms of such
an agreenent should be left up to CB, NEDA and GOP.
Portfolio Quality
27.       IGLF's own STD portfolio has been of remarkably good quality.
This is because when clients default on their loans the sponsoring bank
is still obliged to honor STD repayments. If it fails to do so deductions
are automatically made from its reserve account with the CB to cover STD
payments due. Very little is known about the actual arrears position of
IGLF financed projects, as sponsoring banks do not supply such data to the
CB.l/ During negotiations CB would be asked to collect from sponsoring banks
and compile arrears data on all outstanding IGLF accounts and on all future
accounts on a quarterly basis. While hard data were not available, discussions
with a few sponsoring banks which previously used IGLF facilities indicate
that the default rate (i.e. principal and interest in arrears/outstanding
loans) on their IGLF backed SMI lending was between 30-40% at any given time.
Since they had to meet STD repayments with their own resources (usually
short-term at an average cost of 14%) the direct cost of having their funds
tied up until they recovered arrears due ran in some instances to between
3-4% of their outstanding SMI portfolio. The provision of guarantee cover
in such instances proved to be of little value since the guarantee only
provided protection against actual loss of principal. With the proposal
(paragraph 30) to systematize and strengthen follow-up by sponsoring banks,
it is expected that the incidence of arrearages will be reduced to more
acceptable levels.
Sponsoring Banks Under IGLF
28.       CPA 32 provides that IGLF financing facilities shall be open to
"commercial, investment, savings, rural and development banks and finance
companies, subject to the usual CB regulations". This provision implies
a far greater number of institutions across a wider financial spectrum than
is actually eligible. Various CB circulars and memoranda have been issued
on an ad-hoc basis defining the terms of eligibility and they have in
L/ Furthermore most projects financed by IGLF prior to 1973 have now been
fully repaid and their arrears records are not available. On the other
hand most projects financed since 1973 have not entered the repayment
phase yet and no indicative record of arrearages has been established.
Foreclosure or foreclosure proceedings have been instituted only in
2 instances for IGLF backed projects.



ANNEX 5
Page 11
effect restricted eligibility to the following institutions:
(i) all commercial banks: of which there are presently 36
with a network of 722 branches throughout the Philippines;
(ii)  a restricted number of rural banks:  of which there are
about 690. CB regulations require eligible rural banks
to have a minimum paid-in capital of J150,000 exclusive
of GOP counterpart capital contributions and a class I'D"
credit rating with the CB. Under these regulations
between 150-200 rural banks would qualify;
(iii) all private development banks: of which there are now
32; and
(iv) all savings banks: of which there are 10 with a branch
network of
The list of eligible institutions fluctuates from time to time since the
CB also requires that sponsoring banks operate profitably, maintain
adequate levels of liquidity, have no deficiencies in their reserve accounts,
have no past due accounts either on rediscounted notes or any other special
financing programs, and are regular in their reporting requirements. When
any of these conditions are not met by sponsoring banks they become tempo-
rarily ineligible until the shortcoming is rectified.
29.       Financial institutions which are not presently eligible include:
(i) non-bank financing institutions: e.g. development finance
companies,investment banking institutions and finance com-
panies; and
(ii) savings and loan associations: of which there are 36 all
over the Philippines.
30.       In determining eligibility for access to IGLF the main criteria
seem to have been: (i) whether institutions are subject to normal CB
regulation and supervision and (ii) promotion of the rural banking system.
Little or no emphasis has been given to the extent or potential competence
of financial institutions to adequately service the small entrepreneur on
a sustained basis or to gradually build up a permanent SMI portfolio. The
rationale behind providing access to the present set of eligible institu-
tions has been based on having an initially wide network of scattered
institutions which can submit at least 1-2 projects per year for IGLF
financing rather than a more limited number of qualified institutions which
are capable of generating a much larger number of SMI projects on a sustained
basis.  To accommodate the first alternative with the current rate of
applications will require the building up of a large bureaucracy in the CB
to evaluate and monitor individual projects on a one-step removed basis.



ANNEX 5
Page 12
From the viewpoint of project appraisal, good follow-up and client contact
this is probably a second-best apprcach. The second alternative provides
for the possibility of building up appraisal arnd follow-up capability within
a more limited number of sponsoring banks that could adequately service and
maintain a SMI portfolio.
31.       While there are between 250-280 potentially eligible sponsoring
banks, there were, until 1973, only 34 users of IGLF facilities, 31 of
whom were commercial banks and 3, rural banks. In FY73-74, some 33 insti-
tutions used IGLF facilities; 19 rural banks, 11 commercial banks and 3
private development banks. The rural banks accounted for 19.3% of STD
approvals, commercial banks for 67.7% and the private development banks
for the remaining 13%. Loan applications through rural and private develop-
ment banks tend to generally be much smaller than those through commercial
banks with the average loan sizes in FY73-74 for the three institutions
being P132,000, F325,000 and P424,000 respectively.
Proposed Changes in the Operation of IGLF and the Role of Sponsoring D-nks
32.       As mentioned in paragraph 28, with the current level of operations
IGLF's present mechanism is likely to fail without a substantial augmen-
tation of CB/ILD staff. The build-up of a large centralized administrative
bureaucracy in CB to acdinister an expanded IGLF operations is, however,
likely to prove inefficient and cdrsfunctional. Experience in the Philippines
and elsewhere suggests that the financial needs of SMI are best met through
the greater decentralization of anpraisal, follow-up and risk-taking capability.
TWith this in mind, it is recomnended that changes be made in the administrative
mechanism of IGLF which would shift the appraisal/follow-up functions to the
sponsoring banks (and match thema with the risk-taking function) and allow the
CB to focus its attention on assessing the quality of sponsoring banks' SMI
lending programs and operations.  Specifically this would mean operating under
system of accreditation whereby sponsoring banks, once accredited, woulc imme-
diately be granted STD/G facilities on application and submission of required
documents without further project review. Accreditaticn would be provided on
the basis of sound criteria (Appendix 6), which would ensure the
accreditation of only those sponsoring banks which were financially and
technically capable of lending to &I, in addition to meeting the CB's normal
regulations.  The CB would evaluate whether individual sponsoring institu-
tions met the requirements for accreditation. Each institution's performance
would be reviewed at least once a year to determine whether accreditation should
be renewed.
33.       NEDA and CB, along with the IGLF Review Committee, have accepted
the recommendation that it would be necessary to adopt an accreditation
system for future IGLF operations. However, in order to give some of the
smaller institutions (e.g. rural banks) that are at present using IGLF facilities
an opportunity to meet accreditation requirements, the transition will be
effected over a period of one year.



ANNEX 5
Page 13
In this time, CB has offered to provide assistance to those existing sponsor-
ing banks which do not presently have the full capability required to recruit
and train staff qualified in SMI project appraisal and follow-up. During the
transition CB/ILD will continue to undertake project appraisal and additional
follow-up functions. IGLF is expected to operate fully on an accreditation
basis by June 30, 1976.
Relending Rates, On-lending Rates and Spreads to Sponsoring Banks
34.       The proposed Bank loan of US$12 million (P84 million) will be
re-lent to IGLF by GOP at the prevailing Bank rate of 8.5%. A counter-
part GOP contribution of P30 million will be made to IGLF's permanent
working capital at no cost. The STD rate charged to sponsoring banks
will be 7% and STD's will automatically carry guarantee cover of 60% of the
loan amount. Sponsoring banks will on-lend at 12%, the prevailing statutory
maximum interest rate in the Philippines. The spread on Bank funds will
therefore be 3.5%. However the overall spread of 5% on IGLF funds to
sponsoring banks, while large in comparison with the "normal" spread to
DFCs (3-4%), is considered essential in view of the much higher levels
of administrative costs and risks associated with small industry lending.
An analysis of term lending to SMI in the Philippines (Annex 9) and else-
where indicates that the administrative costs of SMI lending are at least
two to three times higher than for DFC-type lending, while default rates
are about four times higher. The provision of a guarantee to the extent
of 60% does not provide cover against the "losses" due to income foregone
and principal tied-up associated with high defaults. It merely covers
actual losses of principal after foreclosure (a process which can take
several years) and allows sponsoring banks to consider lending to under-
collateralized small entrepreneurs who otherwise have sound projects.
At a minimum, therefore, administrative costs associated with SMI lending
in this instance are expected to be 2.5% of average outstanding loans;
income foregone from delayed interest and principal repayments 1/will
account for a further 1.5-2.0% leaving between 0.5-1.0% as a gross profit
contribution (the average gross profit contribution for financial institu-
tions in the Philippines is about 4-5%). To provide banks with a greater
incentive to develop their short-term lending to SKI, the CB now provides
them with preferential rediscounting facilities (at a rate of 5%) for
outstanding paper with a maturity of less than one year. The 5% spread
for long-term lending would therefore allow sponsoring banks to build up
their long term SKI portfolios without incurring a loss.
1/   Under the proposed Bank loan it is expected that with closer supervision
the default rate will be reduced. This, however, will only occur at the
expense of an increase in administrative costs.



ANNEX 5
Page 14
Other Proposed Changes in IGLF Lending Guidelines
35.       The following matters were discussed and agreed upon at
negotiations:
(i) the loan/equity proportion in project financing would be
raised from 70:30 to 80:20;
(ii)  maximum limits for STD's would be raised from P500,000
to P800,000;
(iii) maximum maturity periods for fixed asset financing will.
be increased from 10 to 12 years with a 2-year grace
period and for permanent working capital will remain at,
3 years with a 6-month grace period;
(iv) non-bank financial intermediaries would be included in
the list of eligible sponsoring banks.
EAP Projects Department

PHILIPPINES
Industrial Guarantee and Loan Fund (IGLF)
Accreditation Criteria for Sponsoring Banks
1.        Financial institutions participating under the IGLF scheme and
eligible for the use of IBRD funds to finance small-scale industries would
meet the following criteria in addition to the Central Bank's normal
criteria (listed below) for credit availment:
1. Minimum paid-in capital of P500,000 (exclusive of preferred
shares).
2. Sound and efficient management and an adequate number of
qualified staff to carry out the institution's normal
business.
3. Capability for satisfactorily appraising the technical,
marketing and financial viability of small industry
projects together with satisfactory systems and pro-
cedures for regularly following up on the progress of
project implementation and operation.
4. An overall level of arrearages (i.e. amounts over 3 months
overdue) of no more than 15% of the total outstanding port-
folio of the institution. Furthermore, in any financial
year actual collections would be no less than 70% of amounts
overdue and amounts falling due in that financial year.
Judgements on the soundness and efficiency of the institution's management,
the adequacy of its staff and its appraisal and follow-up capability would
be made by the Central Bank's Department of Loans and Credits. Accredited
institutions would be required to submit themselves to an annual evaluation
by the Central Bank which would ensure that these criteria continued to be
met before their certification was renewed.
Timetable for Introduction of the Accreditation System
2.        To allow some time to those institutions which presently utilize
IGLF facilities for lending to small industries (i.e. for making loans
above P50,000) and which may not meet the above criteria, to equip them-
selves for accreditation, CB will notify these institutions of the accredi-
tation requirements as soon as possible and will give tniem up to June 30,
1976 to meet them. Until that time CB will continue to receive and approve
applications from these institutions under its present system.



ANNEX 5
Appendix 6, Page 2
Existing Central Bank Criteria
3.        All Financial Institutions Other Than Rural Banks
(i)  The applicant institution must have complied with the
approved program on capital build-up in case such
institution has not met the minimum paid-in capital
requirement;
(ii)  The institution, based on the regular and special
examinations of the Central Bank during the last two
years immediately preceding the date of application
for accreditation, has been operating in accordance with
existing laws, rules and regulations;
(iii) The Management of the applicant institution must adhere to
laws, decrees, general orders and instructions of the CB,
by its Board of Directors and its principal officers;
(iv)  The solvency and liquidity position of the applicant
institution must have been satisfactory during the past
two years immediately preceding the application to
participate in the IGIF program;
(v) The applicant institution has not been penalized for
reserve deficiencies during the last six months immediately
preceding the application to participate in the IGLF
program;
(vi)  The applicant institution must have been operating at a
profit at least for the last two years;
(vii) Required reports are being submitted on time to the
various departments and/or offices of the Central Bank;
and
(viii) The applicant institution must have had no past due
accounts with the CB/IBP either on rediscounted notes or
other special financing programs during the last two
years preceding the date of application.
4.        Rural Banks
(i) The rural bank, based on the general and special examina-
tions of the Department of Rural Banks and Savings and Loan
Associations during the last two years immediately pre-
ceding the date of application for accreditation, has been
operating in accordance with existing laws, rules and
regulations as well as the requirements of the Monetary



ANNEX 5
Appendix 6, Page 3
Board under Circular No. 318 dated April 30, 1971 and
Circular No. 331 dated October 7, 1971 and such other
relevant Circulars and/or Memoranda to Authorized Agent
Banks (MAAB) that the CB may issue from time to time;
(ii)  The applicant rural bank which is authorized to accept
savings and time deposits has no past due accounts with
the CB either on rediscounted notes or other special
financing programs on the date of application or even
if arrearages still exist, such delinquent accounts are
covered by duly approved and strictly complied with plans
of payment;
(iii)  The rural bank's credit rating should be at least Class
"D" at the timeof filing of the application; provided,
that it has not committed any serious infraction of the
laws, rules and regulations governing rural banks,
especially those on rediscounting.

PHILIPPINES
THE NATIONAL ELECTRIFICATION ADMINISTRATION (NEA)
The Institution, its Finances and Characteristics
History, Organization, and Management
1.      The National Electrification Administration (NEA) was established
in 1969 under the provisions of Republic Act (RA) No. 6038. It was re-
organized and expanded on December 1, 1973 in accordance with Presidential
Decree No. 269 of August 6, 1973 which converted it into a corporation,
wholly owned by the government with independent borrowing authority and
corporate powers. Its main functions are to assist with both financial
and technical support, the establishment of electricity and power use
cooperatives in rural areas.
2.      The powers of NEA are vested in a 5-member Board of Administrators,
whose Chairman is Mr. Ramon Ravanzo, General Manager of the National Power
Corporation (NPC). Other members oxf the Board represent NEDA, the National
Irrigation Administration (NIA) and the communications industry.
3.      The Acting Administrator of the NEA , Col. Pedro G. Dumol, has
been at the head of the agency since July 1970. He is assisted by three
Deputies who supervise seven Directorates covering all of NEA's functions
(Chart No.9655). Four Directorates responsible for Cooperative Development,
Finance, Franchises and Regulation of Cooperatives, and Power Use are well
organized and function efficiently, but the remaining three (the Directo-
rates for Engineering, Materials, and Administration) are in need of
improvenent. USAID is financing engineering and management consultants
for a further two years under its third loan to NEA for rural electrifi-
cation. The proposed Bank loan will be handled by the Power Use Directo-
rate (PUD), a well managed, although presently under-staffed department
under the Deputy Administrator for Finance.
Staffing
4.      As of October 30, 1974, NEA's total staff numbered 418, of whom
210 were professionals. The staff is of generally satisfactory calibre.
PUD has a staff of 39, of whom 32 are professionals. Of the professional
staff, 2 are supervisory, 5 are financial analysts, 5 are marketing
specialists, 4 are training and organization specialists, 4,are information
and project researchers, and 12 are technical staff. Present staff levels
are below authorized strength. Management is aware of this and plans to
increase the number of professional staff to the budgeted level of 75 over
the next two years. This is considered sufficient to strengthen work on
the existing projects and should ensure that in future recruitment keeps
pace with requirements.  NEA has also agreed to establish an evaluation
unit within PUD within one year after the proposed loan becomes effective.



ANNEX 6
Page 2
Objectives, Role and Operating Policies
5.      In 1972 NEA was charged with carrying out a program of rural
electrification to provide area coverage throughout the Philippines by 1994.
To achieve this objective, NEA has set intermediate goals of establishing
at least one electricity cooperative in every province in 4 years, and of
establishing a complete "backbone system" of rural electrification within
10 years. NEA,with financial and technical assistance from USAID since
1971, has made an impressive start. By December 31, 1974, 53 cooperatives
had already registered and signed loan agreements with NEA; 27 of these
had partially or fully energized systems, and are now supplying electricity
to 135 towns and villages. Between 1975-78, 23 more electricity cooperatives
are scheduled to be fully energized and are expected to supply power to 300
more towns and villages. Once an electricity cooperative is established,
NEA provides both financial and technical assistance to enable the coopera-
tive to become a viable, financially self-sustaining entity. A co.mlemen-
tary objective of this program is to promote economic development in the
cooperative's service area by implementing ldcal irrigation schemes, based
on electric pump irrigation and by establishing small scale industrial
cooperatives.
6.      NEA's Charter (Presidential Decree No. 269) provides basic policy
directions and stipulates general limits to NEA's jurisdiction. A muLch
more detailed statement of policy is found in NEA's Board Policy State-
ments, which have established clearcut, sound and consistent principles
covering program implementation, appraisal and follow-up standards, and
a number of detailed but essential items such as delineating the duties
and responsibilities of the Administrator.
Financial Position and Performance
7.      NEA's balance sheets for FY73 and 74 are summarized in Appendix 2.
Its present financial position is very sound with a very large paid-in
capital base and the full extent of resources available not yet drawn dotfn.
As of June 30, 1974 NEA had total assets of P295.7 million, of which P158.8
million was in the form of long-term loans outstanding (99% of which were
loans to electricity cooperatives). The significant change in NEA's assets
and capital structure between FY73 and FY74 is attributable to a change from
a public (or Government) accounting system to a corporate accounting
system on July 1, 1973. This resulted in a one-time 250 percent increase in
current assets from P51.1 million in FY73 to P1&3 million in FY74, of which
P30.5 million was in DBP Fund Trustee Accounts._ Total liabilities as of
1/   NEA is required to set up a Trustee Account with the Development Bank of
the Philippines (DBP) for each electric cooperative to which it has made
a loan.



AN1'EX 6
Page 3
June 30, 1974 were P39.1 million, of which P29.2 million was due to DBP
Fund Trustee Accounts. Drawdowns on foreign long-term borrowings totalled
F7.3 million. NEA's net worth was P256.7 million of which P253.9 million
was accounted for by paid-in capital. (Total authorised capital under the
NEA Charter is P1 billion), and the remaining F2.8 million represented
accumulated reserves. The long-term debt/equity ratio was 0.03 and the
current ratio 4.16. Subscribed but unpaid capital stock as of June 30,
1974 was P858 million, of which P482 million represented peso proceeds to
be appropriated from the Government's general revenue and to be paid in
against stock subscriptions over the next five years. A meaningful analysis
of FY74 figures against those of previous years is unfortunately, not
possible due to large differences between public and corporate accounting
systems. Furthermore NEA in its current form has been operating for only
a short period of time and its financial track record has yet to be
established.
8.      During FY74 net income was F2.8 million.  This compares with a deficit
of P2.7 million in the previous fiscal year wlich was offset by a carry-
over surplus of P3.2 million from FY72. The deficit was due to expected losses
in the start-up phase of an operation like the NEA's whose assets could only
begin yielding sufficient income after a time-lag during which operating costs
were necessarily heavy. For FY74 net income as a percentage of average
total assets was 1.2% while the "return" on equity was 1.5% in FY74. Admiii-
nistrative expenses were 1.9% of total assets in FY74 compared to 2.4
percent in FY73; this ratio is expected to drop as operating overheads are
spread over a wider asset base.  As a Government Corporation, NEA is exempt
from all income and other taxes.
9.      The accounts in arrears presently carried on NEA's books are carry-
overs from the pre-1973 Administration which made loans to private companies,
municipalities and a few cooperatives for investment in power-generating
facilities. The new cooperatives established since 1973 do not yet have
an arrears record.  As of September 30, 1974, total arrears on outstanding
loans amounted to P9.2 million (4.6% of outstanding portfolio) of which
P8.6 million (4.3% of outstanding portfolio) was attributable to accounts
over 3 months in arrears. The bulk of the delinquent accounts, over 80%
were over 1 year overdue and were accounted for mainly by municipalities
(58.6%) and by private borrowers (36.8%). Total principal affected by
accounts in arrears was P34.2 million, or roughly 17% of the total outstanding
portfolio. For a public agency, this level of delinquency in its accounts is
not unduly high. In its discussions with the appraisal mission NEA outlined
a satisfactory program of action to further reduce current levels of arrears.
A tabulated summary of accounts in arrears is provided in Appendix 4.
Sources and Uses of Funds
10.     NEA's resource position is summarized in Appendix 5.  As of September
30, 1974, NEA's peso resources stood at P256.7 million, all of which represented
government capital contributions and reserves. NEA's available foreign exchange
resources were US$47.5 million, comprising US$7.5 million equivalent from



ANNEX 6
Page 4
Japanese war reparations collections, and a total of US$40.0 million made
available by USAID. Both these loans are specific purpose loans tied to
cover foreign exchange costs of only the electricity cooperatives develop-
ment program. On both foreign and domestic resources NEA had overconmitted
funds against reasonable expectations that resources would be augmented.
11.     A summary sources and application of funds statement for FY75-77
is shown in Appenidix 6. The principal source of domestic resources will
continue to be government capital contributions (F150 million in FY7'5,
P58.9 million in FY76 and P55.9 million in FY77.) Loan collections and
operating surplus are forecast to provide a growing but small part of
total domestic resources for the next three years in the absence of :Loan
repayments over the period. The bulk of foreign exchange resources will
continue to come from USAID.  As of March 31, 1975 USAID had made four
loans to NEA. The first in 1971 was for US$2.6 million to finance technical
assistance to NEA for the electricity cooperative program. This was followed
by a loan for US$19.4 million in 1972, a loan for US$18 million in 1974
to finance the foreign exchange costs of the electricity cooperative
development program and a loan of $20 million in March 1975. Another loan
of $20 million is expected to be signed in October 1975. NEA anticipates
further loans from USAID to support the electrification program. It is
expected that future loans from USAID will be made on the same terms as
those already made, that, is for a term of 40 years, including 10 years grace
and at an interest rate of 2% for 10 years and 3% for the remaining 30
years. NEA recently (November 1974) signed a loan of US$10 million
equivalent from a group of French banks to finance the purchase of diesel
generating equipment. The loan is expected to become effective in early
1975. NEA's pipeline of projects for FY75-77 is substantial and the
massive inflow of resources will be easily absorbed. The electricity
cooperatives development program will account for more than 90% of all
expenditures during this period. Funding requirements for this program
seem sufficient until FY77-78. On the other hand although industrial and
irrigation cooperatives will account for only a small (but growing)pro-
portion of expenditure during the period, funds for the equipment component
of these programs are deficient by about P16 million.
The Power Use Directorate
12.     The Power Use Directorate (PUD) was established within NEA in 1973
to assist in developing viable programs in irrigation and rural industriali-
zation.  On the industrial side NEA's objective is to establish one or more
small-scale industrial cooperatives in each service area. This is in
accordance with Section 4(i) of Presidential Decree No. 269 which empowers
NEA to make loans "for the acquisition and installation of electrically
powered applicances, equipment, fixtures, and machinery of all kinds for
residential, recreational, commercial,agricultural and industrial uses."
Chart No. 9654 shows the present organization of PUD.  Present staffing was
described in para. 4. One of PUD's principal functions is to supervise
the establishment of industrial cooperatives and to provide them with
management, product on, accounting and marketing assistance.



ANN4EX 6
Page 5
13.     PUD has five functional Divisions for:
(i)  Training and Organization:  this division conducts pre-
membership education courses for prospective cooperative
members; runs seminars and workshops on cooperatives and
their management for the cooperative management teams;
arranges for specific skills training; assists coopera-
tives in preparing all papers necessary for registration
and in obtaining registration of the cooperative with the
Department of Local Government and Community Development
(DLGCD); and assists in organizing the Board of Directors
and various committees of the cooperative.
(ii) Financial Management: is responsible for installation of
accounting systems within a cooperative and assistance
thereafter. It also conducts regular and special audits and
also carries out all loan supervision work. Its staff will
be increased from 5 to 20 over the next year to handle the
increased workload.
(iii) Marketing Support: which does all the initial selling for
a new cooperative and provides information, advertising
and sales financial record keeping.
(iv) Research and Evaluation: this division is still in its
infancy. An evaluation unit will be established in this
Division one year after the proposed Bank loan becomes
effective.
(v)  Industry Assistance:  although not at present a separate
Division, it is responsible for providing a management
assistance team to a new cooperative which assists in
project identification and feasibility study preparation
and then trains the permanent management team in a coope-
rative once operations have begun.
Operations
14.     PUD has established five pilot industrial cooperatives so far.
These are considered in detail in Annex 7. In venturing into an entirely
new area NEA has learnt by trial and error. As might be expected with
this approach results have been mixed, but on the whole>, encouraging. PUD
has identified three areas of weakness in its present operations, which
it is taking steps to overcome. The first lies in its present system of
auditing industrial cooperatives. These audits are not independent and
suffer from lack of objectivity. In the future NEA plans to audit an
industrial cooperative for the first year of operations only and then
arrange for the cooperative to appoint an independent auditor thereafter.
Second , PUD recognizes that the 5-pilot industrial cooperatives have been
overprotected from real business conditions by NEA. To rectify this NEA



ANNEX 6
is now enforcing its rigorous standards for financial, accounting, pro-
duction and sales reporting systems.  Furthermore, it plans to curtail
the inherent subsidr element in its technical assistance by providing
management assistance team for a fixed duration of 6 months only. There-
after it intends to provide consulting assistance on a cost paid basis
and will strictly enforce loan terms and conditions. Third, experience so
far with the 5-pilot projects has shown that NEA in the early phases should
have provided more marketing assistance, particularly in assisting cooperatives
to secure market contracts before operations begin and more assistance in
the procurement of capital equipment and materials, by arranging foor super-
vision of procurement either by NEA staff or by consultants.
15.     NEA's loans have been channelled to the 5 pilot industrial coope-
ratives through local electricity cooperatives. NEA funds are lent lo the
electricity cooperative for 6 years including one year of grace and at an
interest rate of 5%. The electricity cooperative then relends th. >rro-
ceeds to the industrial cooperative for a term of 5 years, including six
months grace and at an interest rate of 6%. In future NEA will make
loans to the electricity cooperative, at 11.75% for maturities
varying between 3 to 12 years, and grace periods between 6 months and
2 years, depending on whether loans are for fixed assets or working capital.
The electricity cooperatives will on-lend to industrial cooperatives at 12%
for the same maturity and grace periods. NEA's intention to raise interest
rates would be confirmed during negotiations. A direct lending relationship
between NEA and the industrial cooperatives was discussed but NEA felt that
it would prefer loans to be coursed through electricity cooperatives for
a nominal fee. The principal reason for this preference was that electricity
cooperatives would serve to provide arn overall unbrella for other NEA financed
power use activities and should retain a ink with cooperatives established
for this purpose. Furthermore NEA felt that identification with the electricity
cooperatives would ensure greater local level support for the industrial
cooperatives.
Power Use Pipeline and Proposed Projects
16.     NEA expects to establish 5 more industrial cooperatives in FY75,
11 in FY76, and 13 in FY77. The proposed Bank loan will finance the 24
industrial cooperatives to be set up in FY76 and FY77. Costs are
detailed in Appendix 8. Total project costs amount to P28.5 nmillion,includ-
ing the provision of technical assistance by NEA estimated at Pl.4 million
over the program period. NEA expects to commit funds for these projects
aver two years FY76 and FY77 and to disburse them over four years FY76-79.
Cooperative members are expected to contribute 20% toward project costs
mainly in the form of land and buildings; 60% will be covered by the pro-
posed loan and the remaining 20% by NEA from its owr. domestic resources.
Disbursement of Bank funds will be against 75% of NEA's own disbursement.



ANNEX 6
Page 7
17.     Nineteen of these 24 industrial cooperative projects have already
been identified (Appendix 9). Of these, two are handicraft/export oriented
projects, two are cold storage projects, four are food processing projects,
six are furniture construction projects and the balance include garments,
tire retreading, a tannery, manufacture of electrical sub-components and
metalcraft. Projects are identified according to a set of weighted project
selection criteria.  The most important criterion is the strength of the
local electric cooperative and local community response. Other criteria
in descending order of importance are raw material availability, labor
skill availability, product marketability, employment generation and the
level of development of the region in question.
Technical Assistance Provided by Power Use Directorate
18.     NEA has provided substantial free technical assistance to the 5
pilot industrial cooperatives. This includes management team assistance,
marketing, production and accountancy assistance. It also includes
indirect costs arising from NEA Head Office support, employee benefits,
office supplies and equipment and other minor items. The cost of such
technical assistance has been about R50,000 per project, of which P39,000
or 78% has been direct costs and P11,000 or 22% indirect costs. Direct
costs have been broken into pre-operational and operational phases. The
pre-operational phase includes technical assistance with the preparation
of a feasibility study, training and pre-operations production run. The
operational phase continues for 6 months after the start of full production,
although in practice experience has varied from cooperative to cooperative.
The pre-operational phase has accounted typically for 45% of total direct
costs per project and the operational phase for 55%. The only technical
assistance for which industrial cooperatives paid fees was for the hiring
of two production experts by two of the pilot projects. Their expenses
were met in full by the industrial cooperatives concerned.
19.     NEA considers that this provision of technical assistance was too
generous both in relation to the budget of the Directorate for Power Use
and because it unduly subsidized the industrial cooperatives (the average
loan size of the 5-pilot projects is P300,000). Consequently, it is devising
a scheme of fee payments, which is designed to make the industrial coopera-
tive pay for about 60% of direct technical assistance received. The scheme
will be so devised that the heaviest subsidy will occur in the pre-opera-
tional phase, with a gradual tapering until the industrial cooperative is
paying in full for technical assistance received. This scheme was
discussed and agreed with NEA during negotiations.
EAP Projects Department
February 24, 1975

ANNEX 7
Page 1 -
PHILIPPINES
National Electrification Administration (NEA)
Industrial Cooperatives Program
Background
1.        In accordance with Section 4(i) of its Charter (Presidential
Decree No. 269), NEA , beginning in 1973, has set up 5 pilot industrial
cooperatives, each in the service area of an NEA electricity cooperative.
This is the first step in NEAts Power Use Program, designed to spur
economic development in the electricity cooperative's service
area by developing projects with a-low capital investment per
worker in order to increase income and employment in the rural areas and
to promote the regional dispersal of small-scale industries. The ob-
jective of the Power Use Program is to establish at least one industry
project in each of the 36 electric cooperative areas being created in the
first stage of NEA's rural electrification program (1974-78).
2.        None of the 5 pilot industrial cooperatives has been in exis-
tence very long and as yet there is no basis on which to make a full
evaluation of the projects. But their establishment and operations to date
have highlighted a number of problems and possible solutions to them which are
enabling NEA to adapt and refine its approach. Problems which relate to
NEA have been discussed in Annex 8 (para. 14). Those which relate speci-
fically to the industrial cooperatives are discussed below. The experience
that NEA has had with each of the 5 coeperatives has been summarized in
Appendices 1-5.
Identification, Establishment and Organization of an Industrial Cooperative
3.        Three of the 5 pilot projects were identified by the local com-
munity and brought to NEA's attention via the local electricity coopera-
tive; one (Capiz) was identified by NEA; and one (Bulacan) was identified
jointly by NEA and the local community. The importance of local community
initiative in bringing possible projects to NEA's attention, and of sup-
porting cooperatives, once established, has been found by NEA to be critical
in the execution of the program so far.
4.        Project identification was followed by a feasibility study in
all of the projects, except the first (para. 14). The local electricity
cooperatives provided information, survey manpower and vehicles, and in
every case the local municipality provided information and assistance.
The feasibility studies conducted so far have generally treated each aspect
of the project superficially and have concentrated on the economics of
production of specific products.  They have not taken sufficient account



ANNEX 7
Page 2
of specific problems in: financing and cash flow; marketing and product
selection; organizational issues, in particular the timing of the transition
period when the NEA management assistance team leaves and the permanent
local managers take over; specific production processes; and training for
both production workers and the permanent local managers. NEA has decided
to upgrade all future feasibility studies, and establish a standard format.
for them. It undoubtedly has the capability to do this, based on its
experience in producing thorough feasibility studies for proposed electric
cooperatives, and the good calibre of staff in the Power Use
Directorate.
5.        None of the projects encountered problems in forming the coopera-
tive. NEA's experience in setting up electricity cooperatives ; the
assistance of the local electricity cooperative in conducting the
information and membership campaign; and local enthusiasm for the
projects accounted largely for this. Two cooperatives were over-enthusiastic;
they started with too many members and had to deliberately reduce membership
to a manageable level. All members work at the project, but non-members
are also hired as workers with the intention of eventually being allowed
membership. The Board of Directors is elected by the members but does not
manage the project. In all cases, the membership fee is a one time payment
of P100.00.
6.        All of the 5 pilot cooperatives encountered considerable dif-
ficulty in registering as cooperatives with the Department of Local
Government and Community Development (DLGCD) in spite of a great deal
of assistance from NEA with filing and meeting DLGCD requirements.
DLGC,D s registration procedures and requirements are complicated and
cumbersome and have been the principal bottleneck in establishing the
cooperatives. NEA's Charter provides for registration of electricity
cooperatives with NEA, and NEA has agreed to seek the same terms for
registration of its industrial cooperatives. NEA provides substantial free
technical assistance in the preoperational and operational phases of production
activity (Annex 8, para.18). The preoperational phase in the case of the 5 pilot
projects has varied between one to four months before operations began.
Technical assistance has continued for up to 15 months in the operational
phase. The quality of NEA assistance in general management, production
and accounting has been satisfactory, but marketing assistance has been
relatively weak. in future, NEA intends to ensure that, where appropriate,
a marketing contract has been secured before operations begin. Where
necessary, it would buttress its marketing assistance with consultants
as it has done for production for two projects, and would deepen the
marketing analysis in all future feasibility studies.
Management and Staffing of Industrial Cooperatives
7.        Staffing experience for the management of the 5 cooperatives
has been mixed. Qualifications and backgrounds of project managers have
varied (Appendices 1-5). Selection criteria and procedures for
filling cooperative management positions have not yet been formalized.



ANNEX 7
Page 3
In each of the 5 cases, a candidate for General Manager was selected
locally with NEA participation in the selection process. To date there
has been no turnover of general managers. NEA is closely reviewing the
performance of all the project managers appointed so far with a view to
improving their performance and, if necessary, replacing them.  The
greatest difficulty has been encountered in retaining competent accountants
to manage cooperative finances. NEA has so far insisted on hiring accoun-
tants with at least graduate qualifications in accounting. Turnover in
accountants has been high. Three cooperatives have lost their trained
accountants while the other two have just recently hired their own
(Appendices 1-5).  In future, NEA plans to encourage hiring of accountants
with satisfactory experience for the work required and who can be easily
trained, rather than stressing academic qualifications. Recruitment of
other managerial personnel, particularly for marketing and production, has
not proven problematic but cooperatives have tended to place undue reliance
on NEA's management personnel, without taking adequate measures to recruit
replacements before the NEA teams are withdrawn. NEA is encouraging all
five cooperatives to acquire a full complement of qualified managers as
soon as possible after establishment to allow for sufficient overlap
during the assistance teams' stay.
E.        For the future, IEA plans to introduce the rigorous and well-
tried procedures it has developed for recruiting management for electricity
cooperatives. NEA selects general managers by competitive recruitment with
the local electricity cooperative's Board of Directors selecting from a
short list of three finalists, preselected by LEA. The general manager
selects the rest of his management team ir accordance with NEA selection
guidelines, which insist on competitive recruitment through examination
and interview. The hiring and firing of a general manager by the local
cooperative Board of Directors is always subject to NBA approval. The
overall management record for the electricity cooperative has been good.
Since 1971 there have been 3 dismissals for dishonesty, one for incomFetence
and one resignation out of a total of 42 cooperatives which have 3ppointed
general managers, of whom 35 have been employed for 6 months or more.
Training
9.        Training of new management for the 5 pilot projects has been on
an "on-the-ob" basis with the new management acting as counterparts to
the NEA management assistance team.  The counterpart general manager has
been under training since the start of the preoperational phase in four
of the projects, and in the fifth was recruited after the NEA management
assistance team has run the project for 9 months. This last experience was
with the second pilot project established and NEA does not intend to repeat
it.
10.       Training of new members of the cooperatives in cooperative organi-
zation and administration is conducted by the Training and Organization
Division of NEA's Power Use Directorate in conjunction with the Department
of Local Government and Community Development (DLGCD). This is a well-
organized operation. The Training and Organization Division is also res-
ponsible for conducting worker skill-training in the preoperational phase.



ANTEX 7
Page 4
The training content itself is satisfactory, but in every project, the length
of time for which skills training was given was regarded. as inadequate on
the basis of subsequent operational exneriernce. NEA intends to lengthen,
as appropriate the skills training period irn future projects and is negoti-
ating with the National Manpower and Youth Council (4I-YC) for that agency
to provide appropriate training instructors to be financed from NEMYC'S
budget.
Industrial Cooperatives' Operations
11.       Operating experience and problems to date have highlighted the
following common problems:
(i) raw materials procurement: has proven difficult
for cooperatives in the textile industry. With a general
shortage of local and. imported raw materials, there is nc'
way to overcome this, and the best expedient for textile
related projects is to rely heavily on sub-contracting
work, at least in the early years of operations;
(ii) capital goods procuremert: mistakes were made in three
instances when equipment was procured without NEA's guidance.
These errors could have been avoided with better procurement
procedures.  NEA has now decided to supervlse carefully, or
else have consultants supervise, procurement for each of
the cooperatives to be established;
(iii)  production problems:  of varying severity have been encoun-
tered in all cooperatives either because of inappropriate
capital equipment or inadequate tr-r.ing;
(iv) marketing problems: were serious in three projects, largely
because product lines were not market oriented.  NHEA's pro-
posals to overcome this problem have been described in
para. 6;
(v) financial problems: which relate mairnly to inadequate
financial planning and problems in retaining accountants
(para. 7).  The inadequacy of financial planning is evidert
in the simplistic financial analysis in NEA's feasibility
studies; and the absence of sufficient forethought about
cash flow at the time of establishment. Working capital
requirements were badly underestimated in two cases, and
this is likely to affect the projects concerned for some
time.  Also, the impact of a short grace a,)( maturity
periods on a project's cash flow, particularly ir the initial
stages of operations, has not been given sufIi cient considera-
tion. These problems have been reviewed by 'HEA and resulted
in its decision to enforce strictly its flnancial reportin.



AlITEX 7
Page )
requirements, lengthen the length of grace and amorti-
zation periods, take appropriate measure to ensure
that projects retain their accountants (para. 9) and
conduct a more sophisticated. financial analysis of the
project in the feasibility study, including a financial
rate of return, together with detailed cash flow and income
statement forecasts; and
(vi) NEA's reporting requirements: NEA requires a monthly problem-
oriented report and financial statement from each project as
a condition of its loan. NEA has not enforced this, nor has
it enforced loan repayment when the six month grace period has
expired.  NEA has undertaken to fully enforce its reporting
requirements and all other loan terms and conditions. NEA
is also developing a standard form of loan agreement with
industrial cooperative bo.rrowers. It already has a standard
form of loan agreement with its electricity cooperative
borrowers.
NEA's Program for Corrective Action
12.       Most of the problems mentioned above have been identified by
NEA's own program of monitoring and review. Its response in taking
corrective action is rapid and as has been described in the preceding
paragraphs, a number of measures have been planned or are being taken
to rectify the situation and improve cooperative performance. Since
this program isstill very much of a pilot nature, NEA feels that its
planning and evaluation capability needs strengthening (Annex 6, para.
13) to conduct better analyses of each cooperatives' problems and to
evaluate the program as a whole in terms of financial and economic
measures. During negotiations, the introduction of an adequate evalua-
tion and reporting system, and the time-table for its introduction,
were discussed and agreed with NEA.

PHILIPPINTES
National Electrification Administration (]TEA)
Industrial Cooperatives Program
THE CAPIZ GARMEIT PROJECT
A. Summary Analysis
1.        This project was the first established by NEA.  No feasibility
or marketing studies were conducted and no accounting system was installed
before operations began. Nor was an adequate and assured supply of raw
material (cotton cloth) found. Initial production was for job lot orders and
was characterized by two-week pr6duction runs, followed by  shut-downs due
to the unavailability of raw materials. In November 1973, 125 new sewing
machines were procured, but the market for the cooperative's products had
collapsed and operations ceased from January to June 1974. To service the
project, NEA sought the assistance of the Development Academy of the
Philippines (DAP). Following a feasibility study carried out in April
197h, the cooperative was reorganized, a permanent general manager hired
and trained and an accounting system installed. Production of linen
(bed sheets and pillow cases) began in August 1974 with a stable supply
of raw materials and a small but steady market. In September, the co-
operative won a trial order to assemble precut children's dresses for a
large Manila export firm with a one-year renewable contract to follow.
Another contract of a similar nature is under negotiation. The project
now has a permanent management team of 8 (including a marketing manager
and 4 supervisors who report to the production manager) and employs 50
sewers. Capital investment per job is about P4,700 which is significantly
above the P1,000 forecast by NEA, but in line with their more recent
experience.  Because the project still does not have an accountant, NEA
had to conduct a special audit in October 1974. The results are not
available yet. The project is now considered to have a fair chance of
succeeding by TEA.

PHILIPPITES
National Electrification Administration (NEA)
Industrial Cooperatives Program
ALBAY FIBERCRAFT PROJECT
A. Summary Analysis
1.        Albay Fibercraft was NEA's second pilot project.  The co-
operative was organized through local initiative in August 1973.
Between September 1973 and November 1974, several contracts with local
exporters were won and a variety of job lot orders received. Pr4oduct
lines have been slightly expanded from the original four and the pro-
ject now employs 660 people, of whom 45 are in managerial or supervisory
position. Worker productivity has increased about 300% since operations
began while labor costs have increased 30% over the same Period. Capital
investment per Job is about P300.00.
2.        The project's principal problem has been to hire and train
permanent management to take over from the NEA management assistance
team. A permanent general manager took over operations in November
197h. The project has lost two accountants since its inception and
a third is under training. The most serious gaps are in production
and marketing where NEA personnel are still in place. NEA and the co-
operative are taking urgent steps to hire and train permanent management
staff.
3.        The project's financial position and performance are in line
with NEA forecasts made at the time of the feasibility study. An initial
net loss of P2,000 in the first month of operations was followed by steady
improvement. Between July to September 1974, net income averaged P1,000
per month. The last complete available financial statement was as of
May 31, 1974, because of problems in retaining accountants as described
above.  Loan releases as of October 31, 1974 totalled P189,000 of which
P40,000 was required for purchase of machinery and equipment and P150,(00
for working capital. The project has experienced recurrent working capital
shortage because of high raw material costs and late customer payments.
The last problem is being tackled by opening domestic letters of credit.

National Electrification Administration (NEA)
Industrial Co-operatives Program
PAMPANGA BAMBOOCRAFT PROJECT
A. Summary Analysis
1.        The third pilot project to be financed by NEA was the Pampanga
Bamboocraft project. The project was initiated by local community members
of the local electric cooperative and approved by NEA after it had con-
ducted a feasibility study.  The management of the project is satisfactory
and the general manager particularly well qualified. The project receives
technical assistance from a qualified consultant, whose services are paid
for in full by the project.
2.        Major problems remain in marketing and production.  Demand was
minimal for the original product lines selected and no firm line of pro-
ducts has yet been established. Production has been slowed because of poor
equipment procurement and by production line bottlenecks at the finishing
stage.  Production has also been slowed by too brief a training period
for workers and an initial high rate of turnover amongst the labor force.
The production problems are now being remedied, there is a relatively
stable workforce of 30 and productivity per man has increased four fold in
five months. The marketing problem remains and is now NEA's principal
concern about this project.
3.        The latest available financial statements are as of June 30, 1974.
Accounts were not up-to-date because a new accountant was unable to keep
up with reporting requirements. The project sustained a net loss of P1,900
in June 1974. In September and October 1974 the net loss was cut reportedly
to about F1,200 per month. This reflects slower sales than forecast in NEA's
feasibility stucy.  Production costs remain stable, with an 8% wage increase
to F8.50 per day being more than offset by declining prices for bamboo.
B. Operational Details
Location: Candaba, Pampanga Province in the service area of South-eastern
Pampanga Electric Cooperative (SEPECO).
Operations: began February 18, 1974
Project Manager: Mr. Ramon Panganiban has a bachelor's degree in business
administration. He owned a bamboocraft business from
1964-70, after which he worked in the tourism industry.

National Electrification Administration (NEA)
Industrial Co-operatives Program
LA UNION WOODCRAFT FROJECT
A. Summary Analysis
1.        The fourth co-operative to be established by NEA, the project was
based on an identification survey carried out by the local electricity
co-operative for power use projects in its service area. NEA then conducted
its own feasibility stucdr and operations began in April 1974 with a labor
force of 17. A permanent general manager and accountant were hired to be
trained under the NEA management assistance team.
2.        The principal problems encountered have been marketing and pro-
duction. No market survey was done and the product lines initially produced
were not customer-oriented. Subsequently, contracts were obtained from a
large retail outlet and a local exporter in Manila for woodenware and wooden
figurines. Production and particularly production cost control remain
severe problems. The project now employs 28 persons but productivity is
about 50% of normal because of inadequate training. Three trained craftsmen
have been hired to train the existing wark force over a 3 month period.
Plant lay-out contributes to poor productivity which cannot be raised without
considerable additional expenditure on building improvements. Another
difficulty is a very poor raw material conversion factor resulting in
excessive raw material wastage. To overcome this, a large band saw is to
be procured. The management assistance team has also considered the pro-
duction of byproducts such as glue, sandpaper, varnish, and filler to cut
down waste. But the costs appear excessive at present levels of production.
3.        The latest financial statements are as of June 30, 1974.  More
recent statements are not available because the accountant had to be replaced.
A new accountant is being recruited but financial reporting to NEA has
fallen behind. The net loss for June 1974 was P1,140 -- the average monthly
loss over the first four months. NEA considers this to be a problem project.
B. Operational Details
Location:            Tubao, La Union Province in the service area Zf La Union
Electric Cooperative (LUELCO)
Operations:          began March 11, 1974.
Project Manager:     Mr. Bienvenido Marasingan has a bachelor's degree in
business administration from Sophia University, Tokyo.
He is 31 years old and his previous business experience
covers ten years, six of which he was the owner/operator
of a Philippine woodcraft company.

National Electrification Administration (NEA)
Industrial Co-operatives Program
BULACAN EMBROIDERY PROJECT
A. Summary Analysis
1.        The most recent of NEA's pilot co-operative projects,Bulacan
Embroidery is also the best planned and executed. The project was identi-
fied in December 1973 by a joint NEA-local electricity cooperative team,
assisted by two Peace Corps Volunteers. The same team conducted a
thorough feasibility study and began the selection of 31 sewers by competi-
tive recruitment. NEA delayed loan signing until a one-year marketing
contract  had been sucaessfully concluded with a large export garment
manufacturer in Manila for the assembly of pre-cut children's dresses.
The marketing contract and the NEA loan were both signed in August 1974.
2.        The project is successful and the problems presently facing the
cooperative are those related to expansion. Management of the cooperative
is still in the hands of a NEA management assistance team of two, assisted
by one Peace Corps Volunteer. A general manager and an accountant have
been hired and have been under training for four months. A marketing
manager is not considered necessary for the present.
3.        Production is satisfactory and output per day doubled in the first
three months. The target is to double output per day again over the sub-
sequent six months. The project has a good quality control record. Quality
control is carried out by the cooperative.
4.        The latest available financial statements were those for the three
months ending August 31, 1974. Those for September were under preparation
while the mission was in the field, and were expected on time by NEA. The
project was very close to break even after one month of full operations and
the preceding training period. The net loss for the period was P350.
B. Operational Details
Location:            Plaridel, Bulacan Province in the service area of First
Bulacan Electric Cooperative (FBECO).
Operations:          began July 22, 1974.
Project Manager:    Mr. Fidel Corral is 47 years old and has a bachelor's
degree in engineering. His previous experience includes
14 years as an NCO in the Philippine Army, 4 years as
a sergeant in the Philippine Constabulary and 7 years
as an Instructor at the Community Development Center
at Los Banos.

PHILIPPINES
DEPARTMENT OF INDUSTRY (DOI)
REGIGPNAL TEC1MICAL ASSISTANCE CENTER-S (RTAC'S)
Rural SMI's Access to Technical Assistance
1.        New entrants into small and medium manufacturing and established
entrepreneurs seeking to modernize and expand typically encounter bottle-
necks in their operations due to "technicalrlproblems on the production side,
in financial management, or in marketing -- or in a combination of these.
Unlike large corporations, the managements of small and medium firms generally
do not have the level and range of technical slkills necessary to diagnose and
follow-up problems as they arise.  Moreover, the time and resources necessary
to seek out the right type of assistance are not avrailable. The need to up-
grade entrepreneurial, management and technical skills and the lack of access
to adequate resources (in the form of personnel assistance, readily available
information, or capital) to bring it about is a problem shared by small in-
dustries throughout the Philippines, in urban 1/as well as rural areas.  How-
ever in rural areas where the general level of development in manufacturing,
commercial services, communications and other infrastructure facilities is
much less advanced, small and medium firms are far more isolated than their
urban counterparts.
2.        To some extent technical assistance to SMI in various stages of
project development is available, for example, through the Institute for
Small-Scale Industries (ISSI), the Development Academy of the Philippines
(DAP), and specialized research centres such as the International Rice Re-
search Institute (IRRI). Other agencies, such as the Food Terminal, Inc.
(FTI), the Bureau of Domestic Trade (BOiT), and the Design Center of the
Philippines (DCP), are also planning to assist SMI with technical services.2/
However, present programs are primarily Manila-based, and there is no central
clearing house where a small entrepreneur can go to find out what services
are available to him in various fields.
1/ International Labor Organization, Sharing in Development: aprogZramme of
employment, equity and growth for the Philippines, Geneva, 1974. See
Special Paper 9.  "Medium-Scale and Small-Scale Industryl' pp. 541-545 on
the findings of the Greater Manila Survey of S,MI which indicate the need/
lack of technical assistance where it would be expected to be of greatest
accessibility in the country.
2/ See Annex 3 for a view of the overall GOP program for promoting SMI and
Volume II, Part 2 on the role of the various agencies.



A&JNEX 8
Page 2
Objectives and Functions of the Regional Technical Assistance Centers (RTACrs)
3.        Critical to the future success of encouraging the growth of SMI,
particularly in areas outside major urban centers, are:
i) the creation of a Central Support Unit (CSU) to coordinate
information on technical services available in varicus fields;
and
ii)  the establishment of a network of extension-type services to
reach SEI in rural areas with direct technical assistance and
with information on what is available (in the region as well as
nationally) a'nd on how to go about obtaining such assistance.
DOI therefore is taking the initiative on both levels in creating a coordi-
nating unit for technical services in its Marnila office and in establishing
a network of  Regional Technical Assistance Centers (RTAC's) throughout the
country for provisicn of technical assistance services to rural SMI. One
of the functions of the OSUl/would be the collection and dissemination of
information on technical services available to SMI to the network of regional
centers. CSU would also assist the RTAC's by providing additional technical
expertise *where needed and working on formulation of additional programs to
fill in gaps in the kinds of technical assistance needed by rural SMI as
these gaps are identified in the field.
J.        The DOI project is for establishment of one RTAC in each of the
eleven regions of the country.2/ Each Center would function as a base for:
i) building up permanent staff and facilities for provision of technical
services to entrepreneurs throughout the region; and ii) establishing an
information center through which local entrepreneurs would be assisted in
locating and contracting more specialized technical expertise available
from other government agencies, commercial firms, or manufacturers in the
region or nationally. In the start-up phase and for the first years of
operations the centers would be modest operations in terms of staffing and
facilities.  As greater familiarity with the specific needs of individual
firms increases, and the industrial development priorities and plans of
the region emerge, the centers would gradually be better equipped to deter-
mine appropriate expansion of their programs and facilities.
1/   The Central Support Unit is provided for in the D0I FY75 budget appro-
priations.  See Annex 4 on the DOI organization structure and relation-
ship of the Support Unit to the Regional ITechnical Assistance Centers.
2/   The proposed IBRD financing is to cover start-up of the first seven
RTAC 's.



ANNEX 8
Page 3
5.        Each RTAC would be established with the staff capability of pro-
viding generalist-type technical assistance in three broadly defined cate-
gories into which most technical problems of SMI fall:
i)  technology:  e.g.,in choice and improvement of production
equipment, increasing utilization of capacity, standardi-
zation of products and quality control, proper maintenance
of machinery, and in efficient plant layout,work organiza-
tion etc.;
ii)  financial management:  e.g., in the installation of book-
keeping and accounting systems, costing, management of cash
flows, and planning; and
iii)  marketing:  of products and services, in market research
and analysis, forecasting and marketing techniques, and how
to go about negotiating with larger firms for subcontracts.
The centers would function as an extension service, with staff members
spending most of their time in the field working with individual firms
in diagnosing problems, providing advice and, where necessary, drawing up
work plans for making improvements. The field staff would also advise and
assist entrepreneurs in gathering information, for example, from suppliers
and buyers located in the region, and would assist, where appropriate, in
exploring new market possibilities, for example, through contracting large-
scale firms on subcontracting possibilities. Where more specialized
technical expertise (or information) than is available in the region is
required by a firm, the RTAC staff would contact the Central Support Unit
in DOI/Manila for assistance.
6.        Each center would have a conference (classroom) facility 'for staff
use in programs that are developed to work with groups of entrepreneurs --
demonstrations, lectures, short-courses, and so on.  However, the centers
initially would not have any machinery or equipment for workshops or produc-
tion demonstration. The RTAC's have been flexibly designed so that plans
for such facilities could be worked out in the future as demand for them is
determined.
Fees
7.        The RTAC's basic services would be offered on a no-fee basis to all
small and medium firms. There is a provision in the DOI  idget for the RTAC
program for hiring of consultants on a short-term basis.SE/ In cases where a
firm decides to use the services of a DOI contracted consultant or where
technical staff from the DOI Support Unit in Manila is brought to the field
to work on a specific project, the entrepreneur may be charged a fee to cover
all or part of the costs, dependinig on the size of the firm. A graduated fee
schedule for such services is being worked out by the DOI staff.
1/   See architectural drawings attached.
2/   See para 18 on provision in the DOI budget of a fund to hire specialized
expertise for short-term assignments.



ANNEX 8
Page 4
The RTAC's Relationship with Financial Institutions
8.        While technical assistance and financial assistance for SMI are
complementary services, the individual firm may not need both at the same
time. From experience in other countries, it is also organizationally
difficult (and often unnecessarily time-consuming in getting subprojects
underway) to coordinate technical assistance directly with financing. The
RTACs, therefore, would not be directly linked in the requirements of their
work program to the MP or IGLF member-banks. (The DDI Medium and Small
Industries Coordinated Action Program -- MASICAP -- which is an on-going
DOI field program in the regions works almost exclusively in helping entre-
preneurs to present projects in "bankable" form to DBP and IGLF member banks).-
The RTACs, MASICAP, and the financing institutions however would work closely
together inasmuch as, on a day-to-day basis in the field, clients would be
identified and recommended by one to the others. This relationship applies
also to the RTAC's work with other agencies.
Location
9.        The RTACs would be located in the regional admiinistrative centers,
which are also major commercial centers . The exception is the
Southern Tagalog RTAC which is to be located in Batangas rather than in
Manila. As the RTAC would provide assistance to small and medium firms through-
out a region, and would also be responsible for coordinating services and
information from other regional sources and from Manila, the administrative/
commercial centers are considered the best location for ease of communications.
In the future, as the RTAC project expands,smaller satellite offices may be
set up in other locations.
10.       Establishment of the first seven centers proposed for financing
under the IBRD loan would be carried out over a period of two years, as indi-
cated in the list of all center locations given below. (Map No. 11420 in
Volume II. of this report).
Year of
Establishment              Region                RTAC Location
1975-76           VII  Central Visayas            Cebu City
XI  Southern Mindanao         Davao City

in IBRD loan)      VI  Western Visayas            Iloilo City

ANNEX 8
