Management, Structure and Staffing
11.       The RTACs would become part of the organization structure of the
Department of Industry, under the direction of the Field Operations Unit.!/
However, as DAP and ISSI have built up experience and considerable in-house
capability in planning and programming technical assistance for SMI, the
DOI plans to contract their assistance in organizing and implemeating the
RTAC project in the start-up phase. Accordingly, the DAP and UP/ISSI would
be instrumental in carrying out preoperating activities for creation of the
centers, in recruiting, training, and fielding staff to the regions, and in
providing follow-up advice in the first year of operations.
12.       The centers would all have the same basic organizational structure,
as illustrated in Chart No. 7444. Each center would be managed by a Project
Director, who would have a small technical staff providing expertise in
financial management, industrial engineering, and marketing. The technical
staff would consist of generalists, capable of applying their expertise to
a broad range of problems in their respective fields. In each area, pro-
fessional staff;would have the support of a technical assistant, with whom
they -wuld work as field teams. The Project Director would be responsible
for overall project selection, coordinating the work of the teams, and for
maintaining the channel of communications to the Manila office for information
and for more specialized assistance.
13.       At the outset, the RTAC Director and his staff would call upon local
government officials, financing institutions, the MASICAP teams, and local
trade organizations to assist them in identifying entrepreneurs who could
benefit from their services. At the same time, in travelling throughout the
region, the teams would, in effect, undertake their own survey of where
assistance is needed most as part of the ground work for drawing up a longer
term work program for the center.
14.       A varied approach would be taken to overcome the normally difficult
problem of recruiting staff with appropriate skills and versatility to work
in the field. A scheme is being prepared by DOI on the basis of recruiting
experienced people from the private sector for short-term appointments,
arranging for short-term secondments from among experienced staff in DAP,
ISSI and other agencies, and combining this staff with new recruits with
appropriate skills, but less work experience. A system of incentives for
permanent DOI staff in terms of higher emoluments for regional assignments
and better promotion opportunities is also under consideration. (An approach,
similar in many respects to the one described, has been successfully adapted
in recruiting for other programs under the direction of the Secretary of
Industry).
1/   See Volume II, Part 2, Chart No. 9359.



ANNEX 8
Page 6
Scheduling and Costs
15.       The proposed IBRD loan would partially finance the establishment
of the first seven centers. Four centers (Cebu, Davao, San Fernando, and
Tuguegaro) will be constructed and come into operation in FY75-76, foLlowed
by three more (Legaspi, Tacloban, and Cagayan de Oro) in FY76-77 (Chart
No. 9443).  Each RTAC is expected to take approximately 120 days to complete
from budget preparation to start of normal operations, as shown in Chart
No. 9445. The centers would be identical in building and facilities; staffing
patterns would also be similar, with the exception of the Cebu center which
would have a staff of 14 (8 professional and 6 supporting) instead of 10
(6 professional and 4 supporting) as in the other centers.
16.       The total budget for the RTAC project is provided in Appendix 1, and
is summarized below:
Table 1: Summary of RTAC Costs and Portion to be Financed by IBRD
Total Costs          TIRD Financing
(F 000's)     (F OOO's)       % Qf Total
Pre-operating Costs              230              115            (50%)
Operating Costs                2,881            1,492            (52%)
Capital Costs                  7,657            3,256            (42.5%)
Grand Total              10,768           4,863            (45.2%)
(Us$ equivalents):             (US$1.5 million) (US$0.7 million)
The proposed IBRD loan would cover about 50% of the pre-operating and operating
costs and 42.5% of capital costs (that portion excluding land and site
clearance). IBRD financing would cover 45.2% of total costs for the RTAC
project for the first two years. The remainder would be financed by the GOP.
RTACs would, thereafter, be supported on a continuing basis out of the DOI
current budget.
17.       Pre-operating costs directly related to each center cover:  (i) salary
and benefits for the professional staff (Project Director, Specialists,
Assistants, and Administrative Officer) for a two month period, including one
month orientation and training in Manila and one month in the field for pre-
paratory work; (ii) transportation expenses to and from the field for the staff
of the centers and the Project Supervision Unit; and (iii) a contingency
allowance of lT% of total pre-operating costs for FY1975-76 and 25% of total
for FY1976-77.1/ The training costs for the one month formal program to be
1/ These percentages are applied for contingency allowances in each budget
category.



ANNEX 8
Page 7
conducted in Manila and the cost of the Project Supervision Unit (DAP/
UP-ISSI) are attributed directly to the Department of Industry budget for
the RTAC project for the two year period.
18.       Operating Costs include the salary and expenses of each center, pro-
rated for the period of normal operations during the first year. (The first
four centers will be fully operational during the second year, FY76-77).  The
costs of consulting assistance, which will be used by the centers as required,
is part of the DOI Head Office operations budget. IBRD funds will finance 50%
of all operating costs excluding salaries and associated personnel costs, but
will cover 100% of the costs of consulting assistance.
19.     The Capital Budget includes the appraised value of land, to be
donated (or leased) by local government. The costs of the Marcos-type
fabricated buildings and construction are taken from the schedule of
standardized costs for the models. The designs for the Marcos-type
buildings are shown in Appendix 2. Each center will be provided with the
standard office equipment and with two vehicles. Local competitive bidding
would be required for the office equipment and vehicles to be procured for
the RTACs.   There is sufficient domestic and international manufacturer
representation in the Philippines for these items. Individual contracts
for these items, considering the phasing of each center, would be too small
to warrant ICB. There is only one domestic source of supply for the
standard prefabricated buildings required for the RTACs, and the nearest
substitutes are over 30% higher in cost. Hence bidding would not be
required for this item.
Expected Future Development and Impact of the RTACs
20.     The proposed RTACs would form the core of a broad network of tech-
nical assistance extension services to be provided to small and medium
industries throughout the country. A recommendation has been made to the
mDI to design and ensure the installation of an evaluation system within
each of the centers so as to have some measure of cost/effectiveness of its
programs and to gather information systematically on the practical needs
of SMI that could be met by additional RTAC services. A network of RTAC
branches within the relatively large regions may be necessary, and additional
facilities such as workshops and demonstration centers may be useful in areas
of high concentration of similar kinds of manufacturing activities. In
taking a sound, pragmatic approach, however, the DOI is determined to
establish sufficient demand (or good potential) for additional technical
assistance before further investments are made.
21.     The technical assistance program, as it progresses, will have the
impact of seeing small and medium entrepreneurs becoming established in
rural areas, using their resources more efficiently, and expanding. Thus,
both investment and employment opportunities in manufacturing and related
services in less developed areas will increase. Moreover, as small and
medium industries tend to be more labor intensive than large-scale manu-
facturing, and as wages (and labor productivity) in manufacturing on the
average tend to be higher than among agricultural workers in the Philippines,
a more favorable pattern of income distribution will be encouraged.
EAP Projects Department

PHILIPPINES
*nall and Medium Industry Development Project.1
Costs, Risks and Spreads in Lending to SMI
1.        Commercial lending institutions have generally tended to avoid
lending to small and medium sized industrial firms because they: (i) are
usually undercollateralized; (ii) are administratively more expensive to
service; and (iii) considered to be a much higher lending risk than larger
clients.  For institutions to lend viably to SMI clients, the interest-rate
margin -(spread) between their cost of funds and lending rates needs to be
higher than for lending to "blue-chip" or established large industrial clients.
Since under the proposed project a scheme was being recommended for Bank
financing which would involve the participation of private financial interme-
diaries of various-types, a brief analysis was undertaken on the size of
spread necessary to provide such institutions with sufficient incentive to
participate extensively in SMI financing.
2.        The Bank in its lending operations with conventional DFCs and
agricultural credit intermediaries 1/ appears to have accepted a spread of
around 3.5% as the unwritten maximum. 2/ While, on occasion, this figure
has been exceeded even for DFC type lending, 2/ special justification is
usually required whenever this spread is exceeded for any type of lending.
This figure of 3.5% has not been based on any in-depth analysis into cost or
risk structures but seems to have acquired legitimacy over time and through
experience.  On the basis of internationt, data comparisons (Table 1) on spreads
for various small loan financing schemes=W and certainly on the basis of the data
available on the Philippines (Table 2) it would appear that a norm of 5-7%, as a
spread for institutions undertaking small industry lending is more appropriate.
1/ In view of the very wide differences between types of agricultural credit
institutions and the variety of ways in which administrative costs and
risks associated with lending operations are covered by governments, it is
very difficult to make any meaningful general statements about the "spreads"
of -these institutions. The Bank policy paper on agricultural credit
indicates that administrative costs for such institutions range from 3-8%
o- azerage outstanding assets depending on the average size and maturity
of  the loan. Total costs of providing such credit excluding supervision
and ancillary services range between 15-20% of average outstanding assets
at any given time.
A :ecerxt policy paper on "World Bank Assistance to Public DFCs" specifi-
cal>y concedes that "operations focussed on small-scale enterprises may
require higher spreads than those traditionally encountered in DFC
lending". The Bank's recent report on "Financing the Development of
Small Scale Industries" echoes this view noting that "available experience
is too scanty to provide a basis for sound generalization as to the
requ-Ired spread but a few examples suggest a plausible range of 4-6
percentage points."
3/  Sri Lanka, Israel, Turkey, Ecuador to mention a few cases.  DBP presently
has a spread of 4% on the recent DFC loan made to it.
/ Data in this area are spotty. The specific features of some of these
financing schemes as to whether institutions cover full cost or risk
are not very well known.



ANNEX 9
Page 2
3.        In attempting to determine the minimy2 spread for financial
institutions that would participate under IGLF-   the appraisal mission
took the following considerations into account:
(i) The administrative costs of lending to SMI: These were
found to be generally much higher than for conventional
DFC lending or for lending to large industries. From the
data available (Table 2) v/ on DBP's Head Office operations,
the administrative costs/average assets ratio was computed
to be 2.35% (about 4 times higher than the 0.60% for its
large loan operations). This is probably a minimum estimate
for the following reasons. First, Branch operations have
not been included (the available cost data were not amenable
to meaningful breakdowns). Inclusion of Branch cost data
would increase the ratio because Branches deal with SMI
clients far less sophisticated in dealing with institutions
than their Manila counterparts, and more widely dispersed.
Secondly, DBP, at present, does virtually no regular follow-
up on its SRI loans. If, as has been recommended, greater
attention is paid to follow-up, administrative costs are
likely to go up further still. With DBP's recent increases
(of about 50%) in its staff for SMI operations the adminis-
trative costs/average assets ratio is expected to rise
between 3% and 3.5% in future years. This is not out of
line with experienceelsewhere. )/
1/  These would include various types of financial institutions viz. the
larger rural and private development banks; commercial banks; non-bank
financial institutions such as DFCs, investment companies, and thrift
institutions.
2/ Good data on administrative costs for institutional lending to SKI in
the Philippines are difficult to come by. DBP is the only institution
which has provided term finance to SIC for ahy length of time and
provides the only reasonably good base for judgement. PDCP (the Private
Development Corporation of the Philippines) launched an SMI program in
1972 as have a handful of commercial banks (but only for short-term
finance). There are no cost data on banks which previously financed
SMI under the IGLF program.
A recent Bank reconnaissance mission to the Mediun Industry Bank (MIB)
in Korea has found that for NIB, this ratio has averaged 4.86% over
the last 5 years. IfIB deals with virtually the same type of clients
as envisaged under the proposed loan. For the CFP in Colombia, also
a recipient of Bank funds for S14I lending, this ratio is 6.2%. The
very few detailed studies that have been done on administrative costs
for SIa lending suggest that they vary from 3% to as high as 19% (in
one case) of average principal outstanding.



ANNEX 9
Page 3
(ii) A much higher default rate than for typical DFC Lendin:
As Table 2 indicates, the default rate on DBP's SMI loans
is over 60% of outstanding principal on such loans, while
76% of the principal is affected by arrears. Comparable
rates for its large loans are 15.3% and 54% whilst those
for PDCP are 2.8% and 12.9%. Although the very high default
rates to some extent reflect DBP's inadequate follow-up,
the fact remains that even with improved supervision the
risk on SMI loans 1 /will be at least 2-3 times as high as
for large loans, due to the vulnerability of small enter-
prises to rapid changes in economic conditions and their
generally higher failure rates. In the Philippines, and
particularly with the DBP, the risk of actual loss of
principal is fairly low since SMI loans are heavily collat-
eralized. But the income foregone from delayed payments of
interest and amortization of principal, along with the high
administrative/legal cost of foreclosure and recovery,
necessitate a higher margin allowance for the risk factor
in S14I lending. Whilst specific data on "losses" due to
income foregone are nearly impossible to extract and break-
out for DBP, 2/ a rough estimate of such losses for DBP
would be anywhere between 3-6% of the outstanding SMg
portfolio in FY74.
(iii) Contribution to profit: IGLF is primarily aimed at mobilizing
the efforts and resources of private sector institutions in
extending assistance to SMI. Consequently an adequate margin
of profit must be perceived by these institutions in under-
taking such lending. In this respect DBP as a wholly-owned
Government institution does not provide any grounds for
comparison. On the other hand PDCP, as a private corporation,
provides some basis for guidance in determining what an adequate
contribMtion might be. In FY73 PDCP's gross profit/average
assets3 ratio was 3.92%. Excluding the allowance for risk
coverage it was 3.5%. Since in the private financial sector in
the Philippines PDCP's profit margins are regarded as "modest",
this figure should be viewed as conservative.
/ Available international data on SMI default rates show that they vary
between 15% and 70% of the outstanding portfolio, with a large cluster
around 70%. Low default rates are usually achieved when loan programs
have been restricted to well established and creditworthy small clients
or when loan supervision has been unusually close.
D/ DBP does not make any specific provision for doubtful accounts on a sub-
portfolio basis.  Its charge against total income for "contingencies"
(which includes reserves for doubtful accounts) in FY74 (on all accounts)
amounted to 2.2% of total income and 0.45% of outstanding loan portfolio.
PDCP's provisions for 1973, amounted to 3.7g of total income and 0.90% of
average outstanding loan portfolio.
31 Gross profit has been used since it subsumes such factors as risk coverage,
contribution to reserves, taxes, dividends, etc.



ANMiX 2
Page 4
4.        On the basis of these considerations the mission is of the view
that a 5% spread with guarantee cover reduced to 60% is not excessive
for the intermediary institutions which will utilize IGLF. In fact, on
the basis of the figures provided above, 5% may be barely sufficient to
convince such intermediaries of the need to incur the incremental expense
of developing and maintaining sufficient and qualified staff to service
this sector with minlmum desired levels of proficiency. Furthermore,
account has to be taken of the fact that with a reduction of the spread
to 5% and of the guarantee to 60%, sponsoring banks are at the same time
being asked to assume a heavier administrative cost and risk burden.
EAP Projects Department
February 24, 1975

PHILIPPINES
SMAIL AND MEDIUM INDUSTRY PROJECT
ESTIMATED ECONOMIC AND FINAYICIAL BENEFITS
Dm, act
1.     An estimated total of 916  sub-projects will be financed by the
DBP and IGLF under the proposed loan together with 24 industrial cooper-
atives under the NEA program. The direct impact of World Bank funds on
total investment in SMI, and on employment and annual output when the
sub-projects are in full operation is as follows:
Impact on SMI Performance
(In 1975 prices, FOOO's)
ADDITI O'NAL:
Capital Investment                       306,o64
Employment Generated: DBP & IGLF           6,503 )12,303
NEA              5,800 )
Annual Gross Output                      476,492
Annual Gross Value Added                 151,017
Annual Returns to Labor                   47,514
Using 1971 as a base year (the last year for which reliable data are
available), the project would mean an increase in total investment of
8%, and the new jobs created would mean an increase in employment in
SMI of 13.0%. Gross output and value added would increase approximately
5% over the 1971 levels, while returns to labor would increase 7%. On
the basis of rough estimates of growth between 1971-74, it is expected
that from 1975 onwards the proposed project would, over the disbursement
period, increase previously modest SMI annual employment growth rates
from 0.5% to 3.0%; output growth from 4% to 7.0% in real terms; and
wage bill growth from 0.1% to 3.0% in real.terms.  After disbursement when
these sub-projects are in full production, output growth is expectea to
increase to 7.5% annually and wage bill growth to 4.5% annually.
Rate of Return
2.        The estimated aggregate internal (financial) rate of return of the
DBP/IGLF subprojects, as a group, is 38.2%. (See Appendix 1). For the 24
industrial co-operatives the aggregate rate of return is estimated to be
31.9%. While individual rates of return cannot be calculated under such a
project, the method used to estimate aggregate costs/benefits, based on past
performance of SMI, would suggest the order of magnitude that can be expected.
This result for SMI is not out of line with the Ranis estimates of 1971 average
rates of return of 23% per annum in large manufacturnng.l/
1/   ILO.  Sharing in Development:  a programme of employment, equity and
.growth for the Philippines. Geneva: 1974. page 570.



ANNEX 12
Page 2
Assumtions
3.        To calculate estimated employment, output and labor return
effects (Appendix 3) simplifying assumptions were made to adjust for an-
ticipated increases in greater use of capital in subprojects and in
increased productivity of capital employed. A corresponding decrease
in the percentage of wages in value added is accounted for. However,
while labor content declines relatively, the new employment generated
would presumably be more productive given additional capital at its
disposal. In order to better use historical output/capital ratios
for estimating returns on new investment, the data available in book
values of fixed assets were adjusted to replacement values including
average levels of inventories (provided for under the proposed project
for financing permanent working capital).
4.     The effects of inflation are taken into consideration on the employ-
ment side, by adjusting the amount of capital required per worker in 1975.
On the output side, however, the evidence available indicates it is reason-
able to assume that a steady increase in costs of production may be more
than offset by the increases in the general price level. 1/ However if
this assumption is relaxed to take into account the effects of the substan-
tial increase in investment in SMI in terms of an increase in production
costs due to greater demand for raw materials and skilled labor and a
decrease in consumer prices due to the improved supply conditions, then the
private rate of return would decrease correspondingly, and social returns
increase.
Indirect Economic Impact
The data available do not permit quantitative measurement of the economic
impact of the project with complete confidence. The nature of the target
group, however, would indicate favorable impact can be expected on a number
of counts. As the funds will be directed primarily to less-developed regions,
the increased growth in SMI will affect a more equitable regional distribution
of jobs and income, in terms of returns to labor as well as to small investors.
In addition, the availability of manufactured goods and services in closer
proximity to local markets, and the competitiveness of a number of sellers,
will benefit the consumer in the form of lower prices. Small and medium
firms, with the increased access to financial and technical assistance, will
have the opportunity to modernize and expand operations, thereby improving
quality of products and cost efficiency. These improvements should also
lead to betiter and lower priced goods and services to consumers in the regions.
6.     Moreover, as the subprojects are found in all of the major industry
product groups, they combine import substitution benefits, in such industries
as fabricated metal products, machinery, and transport equipment, and in-
creased export earnings, for example, from furniture and specialized textiles
and garments.
1/ Relaxing this assumption to allow for a 15% increase in investment costs
and a corresponding-slippage in_ benefits, would yield an l8.5% rate of
return.



ANENX 12
Page 3
Arrangements for Measuring Economic Benefits
7.     Prior calculation of more precise rates of return for an anticipated
916 different subprojects is clearly not possible.  However, to monitor the
economic impact of the project, the mission has recommended that an evaluation
system be implemented, whereby a sample of subprojects would be earmarked for
follow-up in the field by the Business Research Department (BRD) in DBP.  In
addition, data submitted periodically to the Bank as part of normal reporting
requirements would also enable somewhat cmuder evaluations of impact from time
to tlme.  The Information and Planning Unit in the DOI's Commission on Small
and Medium Industries 1/ would also assist in collecting economic data and
conducting periodic analyses.
8.     A project monitoring and evaluation system has also been recommended
for the iegional Technical Assistance Centers.  The direct and indirect impact
of these centers, which wzill provide technical services in a variety of fields
to both new and established firms, on increased employment, productivity, sales,
and efficiency cannot yet be estimated.  The proposed evaluation system is
expected to facilitate planning for future expansion into services most in
demand ard. to derive estimates of the direct impact of such services.
EAP Projects Department
February 24, 1975

NEA Industrial Co-operatives               4.7         .500           .350           .760         27,120                5,800          11,338           8,612      32,584
Methodology:
SMI Economic Characteristics are averages for establishments with 20 to 199 workers.  Historical data provided in the Annual Survey of Manufactures, 1962-71,
are used as the basis for estimating SMI performance in 1975 terms. (See Volume II, Basic Data Tables) Adjustments have been made based on the following
assumptions.
Column 1.  The ratio of book value of fixed assets to replacement value plus average inventories is estimated at .45.  The average real annual rate of increase
in capital intensity from 1962-1971 of 1.6 is applied for the period end 1971 to end 1976. Prices are adjusted on the basis of NEDA National Accounts
fixed capital formation inflator put into 1971 constant prices. Rates of inflation are estimated at 35% in 1974 and 15% for 1975. Thus, 1975 K/L
ratios are calculated: 1971 K/L i .45 x 1.082 x 1.916. Estimated K/L ratio for co-operatives was provided by NEA.
Column 2.  The ratio of gross value added to fixed assets at book value is adjusted to reflect replacement value plus average inventories and the average annual
rate of increases in capital productivity, based on the 1962-71 real rate of 1.02. The calculation is 1971 0/K x .45 x 1.051.
Column 3.  The ratio of gross value added to gross output is assumed to remain approximately the same.  Ratios for co-operatives is averaged of ratios for
groups in which investment will take place.                                                                                   7  a
Column 4.  1971 ratios of wages to gross value added are adjusted to reflect the average annual rate of decrease of 2.27 for 1962-71 and continuing in the
end 1971 to end 1976 period.  The calculation is 1971W/O x .867.
New Investment includes WB loan proceeds and the financing institutions'and entrepreneurs'contributions.
Benefits are estimates of direct employment impact and annual output when all projects are in full operation.
EAP Proiects Department
February 24, 1975
